Bank Leumi to launch online bank Pepper in US

Laura Noonan, in New York 
492 words
19 November 2018
05:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Israeli disrupter sees opening in high fees charged by American lenders

Israeli-listed Bank Leumi is in talks with potential partners about launching its acclaimed online-only bank Pepper in the US, in what would mark the latest challenge to the high fees that America s main street lenders have enjoyed for decades.

Launched in mid-2017 to  disrupt from the inside , Pepper has won praise for its technology and is opening more accounts per day than its parent bank does across its 250 bricks and mortar branches in Israel.

 We said we wanted to go overseas and we searched wide,  Rakefet Russak-Aminoach, Bank Leumi s chief executive, told the Financial Times in New York, adding that the desire to expand was influenced by inquiries from foreign banks and potential partners.

After looking at markets including the UK and India, the US was chosen as the first international venture because of its size and because  (US banks) are very advanced in some areas but in some aspects they re so behind in basic banking , Ms Russak-Aminoach said.

She is far from the first person to spot an opportunity in a market where big banks expensive branch networks are supported by layers of charges for everything from wire transfers to ATM cash withdrawals.

Goldman Sachs jumped into the US online banking market two years ago with the launch of Marcus, Barclays is aiming to have a full online US bank by next year, and some regional banks including Capital One have created online spin offs.

Pure online banks such as Ally are also trying to undercut traditional banks, while fintechs like share trading platform Robinhood are planning to offer banking services.

Ms Russak-Aminoach said Pepper planned to be a scale player and would partner with a company that already has a large national US client base.

 We 	. 	. 	. 	are currently pursuing several options for collaborations in the US with significant and interesting players,  she said, declining to reveal their identities.

She said it was too early to speculate on how much funding the US entity would have, or the financial specifics, but Bank Leumi would only hold a minority stake in the entity. Bank Leumi s shareholders  capital is around $10bn and it has assets of NIS 450bn ($120bn).

Ms Russak-Aminoach argued that Pepper s technology will set it aside from the competition.  While most digital banks today are simply a new interface built on top of old legacy systems, Bank Leumi decided to deliberately create a self-disruption by building a fully-mobile, digital bank from the ground up,  she said.

A key feature is artificial intelligence which  allows us to get to know our customers, customise relevant content and offer them a personalised banking experience that differs from person to person   one that is completely different from the traditional banking language we have come to know.

Features
Trillion-dollar asset managers hog investor inflows

295 words
19 November 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Asia
16
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

CHART TO START THE WEEK

Just 10 investment managers captured about 90 per cent of last year's investor inflows into the asset management industry in North America as many small and medium-sized players struggled to attract business.

Investors globally directed about $2tn of new money last year into the investment industry, swelling worldwide assets under management to a record $88.5tn, according to McKinsey, the consultancy.

In spite of this benign backdrop, more than half of the North American-based managers that oversee assets of between $300bn and $1tn registered outflows last year.

In contrast, the biggest players, those with more than $1tn in assets, captured a disproportionate share of the new business. BlackRock and Vanguard, the world's two largest managers, both attracted record inflows last year, helped by the shift by investors into low-cost index tracking funds.

A growing number of pension schemes and other institutional investors is also abandoning traditional active funds in favour of concentrating assets into relationships with key strategic partners.

Many mid-sized managers try to offer "all things to all clients" but often lack the scale to match the breadth and depth of their larger rivals.

Onur Erzan, a partner in McKinsey's asset management practice, said those "stuck in the middle" had to choose where to compete according to their strengths. Building new data and analytics capabilities through alliances with technology companies could also help mid-sized players.

McKinsey said the need to build bigger companies that can compete with the trillionaires   managers with more than $1tn in assets   would drive more mergers and acquisitions activity. "Size alone does not determine destiny but the importance of scale will only grow in coming years," said Mr Erzan.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in the UK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors.

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Business
Insurers grapple with increasing wildfire risk

OLIVER RALPH;ROBERT ARMSTRONG 
958 words
19 November 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Asia
14
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Financials. Natural catastrophes Changed conditions push sector to update models and pricing policies for California

It has been a terrible period in California. Devastating wildfires have killed at least 71 people and destroyed 12,000 buildings, making them among the most damaging fires to have hit the state.

The fires have also left insurers scrambling to work out how to deal with a threat that is rapidly becoming one of the most serious   and unpredictable   that they face.

The Camp and Woolsey fires that have been burning for ten days come just three months after the Ranch and River fires consumed about 300,000 acres in the north of the state and less than a year after the Thomas fire destroyed more than 280,000 acres and 1,000 structures in Ventura and Santa Barbara.

Mark Bove, natural catastrophe solutions manager at Munich Reinsurance America, said: "In general, overall wildfires in the US have gone down over the last 30-40 years, but what we've seen is a shift in severity   fires are more severe than they used to be."

There were two reasons fires have become more serious, Mr Bove said. One of them was climate change.

"California has drier conditions, and winter rains are coming later and later. So things are bone dry and can ignite very quickly."

The other is California's rising population and its distribution.

"We are building in areas that are more prone to fire risk and, because 90 per cent of fires have a human cause, you create more opportunities for wildfires to occur in these areas." Chris Folkman, a product manager at Risk Management Solutions, agrees.

"We are building a lot of houses in the wildland-urban interface. The increase of residence [in the interface in California] between 1990 and 2010 was 40 per cent, and of course it has only grown since.

"Typically, that's a nice suburban neighbourhood with a trail head at the end of the cul-de-sac   a desirable place to live."

Mr Folkman adds another risk: US firefighting practice, which has followed a policy of suppressing even small forest fires, which lets underbrush   fuel   build up.

That policy took hold in the early years of the 20th century, after a devastating series of fires in Washington, Idaho and Montana. In recent decades policy has shifted away from comprehensive suppression.

"There is an argument to be made that more smaller fires would have reduced the number of severe fires we see now," he said.

Paul Krump, executive vice-president of insurer Chubb, said: "No doubt wildfires are a significant peril in California. What used to be considered benign risks are in play today. We are always updating our models for that."

Those models, however, are not as advanced as they could be. The biggest risk facing US insurers has traditionally been windstorms on the east cost, so that is where the analysis has been focused.

Sridhar Manyem, director of industry research and analytics at AM Best, said: "The maturity of models for wildfires is not as good as hurricane models. Stochastic models are not as robust as for other perils but insurers can improve their stress testing, making sure they are not too concentrated in one area."

Mr Folkman of RMS said: "Insurers' own loss data is insufficient to quantify today's risk. There has been a string of record-breaking events, there is no precedent, no applicable historical data." So insurers must depend on computer simulations that take today's changed conditions into account.

Along with new risk models, there will have to be new pricing policies.

"These large and intense catastrophic wildfires will have to work their way into the industry pricing in California," said Mr Krump of Chubb. "Prudent underwriters are looking at the return periods differently in California. It's just common sense."

But in many western states, rate increases are highly regulated. In California, insurers must apply to the Department of Insurance for rate increases, a process that can take from three months to a year.

A 1988 law, Proposition 103, requires that companies justify the price increases with loss data and allows customer groups to present their objections.

Nancy Kincaid of the CDI said that "in many cases insurers do not get what is requested   there is a back-and-forth between our actuaries and the companies".

Pricing is not the only way insurers can protect themselves. They can also make sure that they put the right valuations on houses in fire zones.

Replacement costs awere much higher when whole areas were wiped out   the demand surge for tradespeople, and the logistical difficulty of working in burnt-out areas, could both push up the cost of rebuilding, Mr Krump pointed out.

However, wildfires are different from, for example, hurricanes, in that more can be done to ameliorate the damage they do, and in some cases prevent them.

Sean Burgess, chief claims officer at USAA, said his company offered significant discounts on homeowner policies for those who lived in towns that had received fire safety certification under a programme known as Firewise, run by the National Fire Protection Association.

USAA, like other companies, sends fire protection experts into the field to remove risks   such as underbrush or wooden patio furniture   around customers' homes, going so far as spraying fire-suppressant gel when fires are nearby.

Mr Burgess said: "You can't change the path of a hurricane" he said, "but you can change the path of some wildfires, and we do believe some of them can be prevented."

Paragon Coin Inc. and CarrierEQ Inc., which conducted unregistered coin offerings, each agreed to pay $250,000 in civil penalties and to notify investors they are eligible for refunds if they still own the token or can show they sold it at a loss. Roof's Take: Resistance Softens to Selling in Secondaries

By Katie Roof

When is it OK for a founder to sell shares?

Once viewed as a negative signal, the act of "taking money off the table" seems more like the norm. For late-stage startups, secondary transactions have become so commonplace that it is almost expected that employees will be able to sell shares before a liquidity event like an acquisition or an IPO.

GGV Capital's Jeff Richards, for one, has noticed the change, though he doesn't find it concerning.

"When I started my first company in 1997," quips Mr. Richards, "if you raised your hand as a founder and said you wanted liquidity you would get shot."

More in Roof's Take.

Oracle Corp. said it would acquire Talari Networks Inc., a provider of software-defined wide-area network technology, for an undisclosed amount. The San Jose, Calif.-based startup was backed by investors including Four Rivers Group, Menlo Ventures, Silver Creek Ventures and Square 1 Bank.

PrecisionAccess provides access control across internal networks, clouds and external users. Campbell, Calif.-based Vidder is backed by LDV Partners, Presidio Ventures, Onset Ventures and Voyager Capital.

Insurers grapple with increasing wildfire risk

Oliver Ralph in London and Robert Armstrong in San Francisco

Fire disasters take their place alongside hurricanes as  major perils 

It has been a terrible period in California. Devastating  wildfires have killed at least 79 people and destroyed 13,000 buildings, making them among the most damaging fires to have hit the state.

The fires have also left insurers scrambling to work out how to deal with a threat that is rapidly becoming one of the most serious   and unpredictable   that they face.

The Camp and Woolsey fires that have been burning for ten days come just three months after the Ranch and River fires consumed about 300,000 acres in the north of the state and less than a year after the Thomas fire destroyed more than 280,000 acres and 1,000 structures in Ventura and Santa Barbara.

There are two reasons why fires have become more serious, Mr Bove said. One of them is climate change.  California has drier conditions and winter rains are coming later and later. So things are bone dry and can ignite very quickly. 

The other is California s rising population and its distribution.  We are building in areas that are more prone to fire risk and, because 90 per cent of fires have a human cause, you create more opportunities for wildfires to occur in these areas,  he said.

The growing number of fires are costly for insurance companies.Last year s wildfires helped to push up losses incurred by homeowner and farm owner insurers from $4bn in 2016 to $16bn in 2017 according to rating agency AM Best. The Carr fire earlier this year cost $2bn on its own. Although it is still too early for reliable estimates of the cost of the latest fires, early indications are that it could be well in excess of $5bn.

Paul Krump, executive vice-president of insurerChubb, said:  No doubt wildfires are a significant peril in California. What used to be considered benign risks are in play today. We are always updating our models for that. 

Those models, however, are not as advanced as they could be. The biggest risk facing US insurers has traditionally been windstorms on the east cost, so that is where the analysis has been focused.

Sridhar Manyem, director of industry research and analytics at AM Best said:  The maturity of models for wildfires is not as good as hurricane models. Stochastic models are not as robust as for other perils but insurers can improve their stress testing, making sure they are not too concentrated in one area. 

 Insurers  own loss data is insufficient to quantify today s risk,  said Mr Folkman of Risk Management Solutions.  There has been a string of record-breaking events, there is no precedent, no applicable historical data,  he said, so insurers must depend on computer simulations that take today s changed conditions into account.

Along with new risk models, there will have to be new pricing policies.  These large and intense catastrophic wildfires will have to work their way into the industry pricing in California,  said Mr Krump of Chubb.  Prudent underwriters are looking at the return periods differently in California. It s just common sense. 

But in many western states rate increases are highly regulated. In California, insurers must apply to the Department of Insurance for rate increases, a process that can take from three months to a year. A 1988 law, Proposition 103, requires that companies justify the price increases with loss data and allows customer groups to present their objections. Nancy Kincaid of the CDI said that  in many cases insurers do not get what is requested   there is a back and forth between our actuaries and the companies .

Pricing is not the only way that insurers can protect themselves. They can also make sure that they put the right valuations on houses in fire zones. Replacement costs are much higher when whole areas are wiped out   the demand surge for tradespeople, and the logistical difficulty of working in burnt out areas can both push up the cost of rebuilding, Mr Krump pointed out.

However, wildfires are different from, for example, hurricanes, in that more can be done to ameliorate the damage they do, and in some cases prevent them.

Sean Burgess, chief claims officer at USAA, said his company offers significant discounts on homeowner policies for those who live in towns that have received fire safety certification under a programme known as Firewise, run by the National Fire Protection Association. USAA, like other companies, sends fire protection experts into the field to remove risks   such as underbrush or wooden patio furniture   around customers homes, going so far as spraying fire-suppressant gel when fires are nearby.

 You can t change the path of a hurricane  he said,  but you can change the path of some wildfires, and we do believe some of them can be prevented.

Amazon   rebuild Paradise and make it HQ2 / From Bruce Nichol, San Francisco, CA, US

Fidelity Names Steve Neff Head of Asset Management Business; A 22-year veteran of the financial services firm, Neff has worked in technology roles

By Justin Baer 
483 words

The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Fidelity Investments named the company's technology chief as its next head of asset management, the latest sign of the firm's shift away from its stock-picking roots.

Steve Neff succeeds Charles Morrison, who's retiring this December after 32 years with the firm, Fidelity Chairman Abigail Johnson wrote Friday in a memo to employees.

Mr. Neff, who joined Fidelity 22 years ago, has held a series of technology roles in asset management. He was chief information officer of the division until 2009, and since 2013 served as the firm's head of technology and global services.

Mr. Morrison, a former bond manager, had run the division for the past five years. As head of asset management, Mr. Morrison expanded the firm's lineup of exchange-traded funds and other low-cost investments. In August, Fidelity became the first manager to offer no-fee index funds.Fidelity announced his retirement last month.

Fidelity hasn't always picked a former investor to run the asset-management business and has chosen a technology leader once in the past. Mike Wilens, a one-time technology chief, ran the business a decade ago.

But Mr. Neff's rise reflects Fidelity's increasing move from a mutual-fund giant synonymous with stock and bond pickers to a firm that can offer products across an investor's portfolio.

Mr. Neff, 66, is a frequent writer on LinkedIn, the social-media network, where his articles cover topics ranging from leadership and workplace diversity to artificial intelligence. "Given the accelerated pace of advancements in our technology world today," Mr. Neff wrote in September, "we all need to become more at ease with change and rising expectations for digital literacy."

Fidelity isn't alone in changing as the business of managing other peoples' money broadly follows other financial services on a path toward increased digitization. The emergence of electronic trading, exchange-traded funds, computer-driven investing and data science have required each generation of industry executive to be more technology-savvy than the last.

"It's an opportune time for Steve to lead Asset Management, as technology is becoming increasingly important," Ms. Johnson wrote in the memo.

Fidelity's investment management business oversees more than $2 trillion in assets. While the business was once Fidelity's flagship, many younger clients may know the firm best for its brokerage services or 401(k) retirement platform.

"Throughout Fidelity's history, our most successful and impactful leaders have often been those who have assumed different roles in various parts of the firm," Ms. Johnson wrote. "Diversity of experience is something I have always valued because of the unique competitive advantages and interconnectedness of Fidelity's businesses."

Nvidia: tales from the crypto

411 words
16 November 2018
03:37
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Fall in cryptocurrency prices leaves chipmaker with inventory overload

Nvidia backers like to argue that the chipmaker deserves to replace Netflix as the N in the big, fast-growing Faang group of tech stocks. Not only is it a leader in graphics chips used for gaming but its products power some of the most transformational developments in computing, including artificial intelligence and machine learning. The trouble with signing up to the hottest trends is being left out of pocket when the hype dies down.

Cryptocurrency miner demand for Nvidia chips raised revenue last year. But the decision to stock up on the cards was a bad one. As prices for cryptocurrencies such as bitcoin fell, so did demand.

Nvidia s core gaming unit, which sold the cards used for crypto-mining, still makes up the bulk of revenue. The drop in demand means total revenue for the third quarter looks disappointing at $3.8bn. The 21 per cent increase on last year is puny against the usual 50 per cent plus quarterly growth that has powered the stock price.

More worrying is a 7 per cent year-on-year fall in revenue forecast for the next quarter. If correct it will mark the company s first revenue decline in more than five years.

Nvidia thinks its  crypto hangover  will be temporary   and it will. New gaming chips released recently will find a bigger audience next year. It is still investing heavily in R&D too   which should mean new revenue streams to come. Operating expenses are up nearly 30 per cent on last year.

A sell-off since October along with the rest of the tech sector, plus the after hours share price fall means Nvidia s stock is trading at about 20 times expected earnings. That is still more than Intel, the world s biggest semiconductor company, which trades at 11 times forecast earnings. The long-term potential for growth is unchanged. Nvidia has more exciting opportunities than its peers.

But it is going to take longer than one rough quarter for inventory to go back to normal. If crypto prices stay low then miners may well try to sell their own chips. Even at lower prices, Nvidia s inventory backlog is going to prove hard to shift in just three months.

Investors Chronicle: Experian, FirstGroup, Land Securities,

Investors Chronicle reporters 
956 words
16 November 2018
22:20
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Companies analysis from our sister publication

Buy: Experian (EXPN)

Free cash flow rose by almost a fifth, while net debt remained at about 2.2 times adjusted cash profits, writes Emma Powell.

Experian s consumer services division has been the poor relation to its expanding business-to-business operations in recent years   the first half may represent a turning point. Organic revenue growth for the former came in at 5 per cent   compared with a 5 per cent contraction during the last financial year   as efforts to broaden and improve the product range started to pay off.

Management expects momentum in organic revenue generation to continue, anticipating full-year growth for the information group at the top of the 6 to 8 per cent guided range.

However, business-to-business operations continued to be the main driver of organic sales, with Europe, the Middle East and Africa/Asia Pacific leading the way. It launched big data platform Ascend   which provides clients with access to historic credit data   into those regions and pushed further into India, South Africa and south-east Asia. Contract wins for credit services and software in the utilities and banking sector helped boost like-for-like revenue by 5 per cent in the UK and Ireland.

A sharp devaluation in some Latin American currencies against the US dollar wiped 11 per cent off sales in the region and damped the group s statutory pre-tax profits. Despite the economic uncertainty in Latin American economies, the division still managed 4 per cent like-for-like sales growth at constant rates.

Analysts at JPMorgan expect pre-tax profits of $1.2bn during the year to March 2019, giving earnings per share of 99 cents (from $1.2bn and 92 cents in 2018).

Hold: FirstGroup (FGP)

Cash flow more than doubled over the period, thanks to lower interest payments and the timing of certain working capital commitments at First Rail, writes Julia Faurschou.

As the proliferation of cheap flights makes long-distance coach travel unappealing, FirstGroup s Greyhound bus service continues to suffer.

In May, former chief executive Tim O Toole said the transport group would take a  new approach  to this part of the business, with a full external review already under way. Six months later, a 28.5m charge relating to Greyhound s exit from western Canada has further damaged half-year profitability.

But new chief executive Matthew Gregory said now the  lossmaking  Canadian operation has closed, Greyhound s performance should improve. In the meantime, disposal proceeds from bus station properties there could also help make up some of the shortfall.

The First Student school bus brand and First Bus city routes were the main drivers of growth during the first half, with adjusted operating profit up 114 per cent and 57 per cent at constant currency, respectively. First Student retained 92 per cent of its contracts during the most recent bidding season and exceeded management s expectations on new business wins. Meanwhile, First Bus benefited from a review of its routes and timetables, although lower high street footfall hurt passenger numbers, as fewer people take the bus into town centres.

FirstGroup s trains division, FirstRail, includes four weeks of trading from the South Western Rail (SWR) franchise, with sales up 80.7 per cent to 1.22bn   a 5.5 per cent like-for-like increase excluding SWR. But management said industry conditions remain  very challenging  with macroeconomic uncertainty, infrastructure upgrades and industrial action causing widespread disruption. An independent review is under way to determine how to return SWR to profitability.

Analysts at Liberum expect non-rail divisions to offset a tougher period for rail, leading to improved operating profit at group level. For now, the broker expects pre-tax profits of 214m for the year to March 2019, giving earnings per share of 13.2p (from 197m and 12.3p in full-year 2018).

Hold: Land Securities (LAND)

Net debt was flat at 3.7bn, but this represents a relatively comfortable loan-to-value ratio of 26.2 per cent.

Given the tough conditions facing the retail sector, Land Securities did well to minimise the effects on its retail portfolio. In valuation terms, shopping centres and shops fell 2.9 per cent, and retail parks by 4.5 per cent. By contrast, and against all the bearish headlines, London offices were revalued by 0.2 per cent in the six months to September 2018. Overall, the combined portfolio lost 1.4 per cent in value to 1,384p per share on an adjusted basis.

While the retail side remains vulnerable to changes in the way that consumers shop, the London office market is performing well; so much so that the company has a potential 2m sq ft in the development pipeline, representing a total development cost of 2bn. There are also plans to enter the small, but fast growing flexible office market, with 36,000 sq ft of space in Victoria Street, London, to be launched next year. Plans are also under way for mixed use developments on its suburban London retail sites including over 4,000 homes, with planning applications expected to be submitted in the first half of 2019.

Analysts at Peel Hunt are forecasting adjusted net asset value (NAV) at the March 2019 year end of 1,353.7p (from 1,402.7p in 2018).

The office market and housing construction look in far better shape than retail. But even with the shares at a near 40 per cent discount to NAV, current uncertainty limits the upside.

WSJ Pro
Protect Data by Untangling Your Web of Business Partners

1,147 words
16 November 2018
12:56
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Cyber Daily: Protect Data by Untangling Your Web of Business Partners

By Kim S. Nash

Good day. Forget third-party risk. Consider fourth- and fifth-party risk. Companies that outsource storage or processing of corporate data can lose track of where their information is if they don't understand the web of contractors and subcontractors in a modern business supply chain. But vigilance is necessary if data is to remain protected, reports WSJ Pro Cybersecurity's Catherine Stupp.

Necessary, sure, but by no means easy. Executives from Barclays PLC and TD Ameritrade Holding Corp. share some tips about tools and procedures that can help.

**********

Companies Struggle to Track Down Data in a Maze of Subcontractors

By Catherine Stupp

Security professionals can struggle to figure out where their company's data goes once it leaves their systems and it can be an opaque process to find out what vendors their third-party suppliers hire. Continuous inventory work and automated tools to track data could help clarify risks in outsourcing data services, experts say.

"When you have issues with identifying your third parties, there's a good chance you haven't identified all your fourth parties. And this is where it starts to get messy," Michael Beck, head of global supplier assurance at Barclays PLC, said during a panel discussion at the Infosecurity North America conference Thursday in New York.

"One of the challenges regulators always put on banks is,  Do you know where your data is?' And the answer is generally,  We think so,'" Mr. Beck said.

External service providers have emerged as a major security vulnerability. Third-party firms were responsible for 42% of data breaches reported over the last year, according to a Ponemon Institute survey of 1,000 U.S. and U.K.-based technology and risk managers, which was published in November.

Mapping how data travels

Knowing which vendors hold a company's data is especially challenging for large firms that work with many third-party service providers, which in turn partner with subcontractors. In addition to identifying where data ends up, companies should rate the risks of their vendors based on the services they provide and determine if they are critical to business operations, said Frank Roppelt, senior manager for security policy and vendor risk at TD Ameritrade Holding Corp.

Those assessments should look at criteria including how much company information vendors hold, whether they're connected to a firm's systems and applications, and whether they use cloud services, he said, speaking at the conference. Companies also should update continuously their records of which vendors perform specific services, he said.

Barclays' supply chain unit asks vendors to identify which subcontractors receive the company's data. The team visits those fourth- and fifth-party suppliers and does penetration tests against them, Mr. Beck said.

Tools from security firms can give supply-chain managers an insight into their vendors' business partners, Mr. Roppelt said. These include SecurityScorecard Inc., which offers a risk assessment service focused on third-party vendors, and Bitsight Technologies Inc., which provides a vendor management service that sends companies daily security updates.

To find vendors' business partners, some tools analyze the traffic of IP addresses and domain names, he said. Companies can then use that information to ask service providers to specify which subcontractors hold their data.

Beyond paper files

Supply chain and security managers could start their risk assessment by distributing questionnaires to their own employees in different business units. That would help them better understand departmental needs to outsource services. Security teams then can assess the risks of allowing outside access to corporate data, according to Mr. Roppelt.

"Understanding that factor of inherent risk and applying that level of due diligence is something that the industry still needs to work on," he said.

Over time, Mr. Beck has improved his methods of keeping inventories of where data is located.

When he worked at another company around a decade ago, Mr. Beck said, he received an order from the Treasury's Office of the Comptroller of the Currency and was unable to name the vendors his employer worked with.

"We literally had to go dig in file cabinets of all these people to go find contracts," he said.

Percentage of malicious cyberattacks in which a law enforcement action is taken against the perpetrators, according to Third Way, a non-profit think tank

More Cyber News

Fake fingerprints outsmart biometric readers. Researchers at New York University and Michigan State University trained machine-learning algorithms to produce so-called Deep MasterPrints that look enough like real fingerprints to unlock devices. Some biometric readers verify only part of a fingerprint and therefore are tricked more easily, the researchers said in a paper presented Thursday at a security conference in Los Angeles. "Demonstrating flaws in existing authentication systems is considered to be an important part of developing more secure replacements in the future," writes The Guardian.

Mueller indictment against Russian troll firm proceeds. A judge in U.S. District Court in Washington D.C. on Thursday rejected a request by Concord Management and Consulting to dismiss an indictment filed by U.S. Special Counsel Robert Mueller, The Hill reports. The Russian company is fighting accusations filed in February that it conspired to defraud the U.S. government and interfered in the 2016 presidential election.

Kids' connected watch is easily hacked. The MiSafes watch, which uses GPS to monitor the movements of children who wear it, doesn't encrypt data or secure user accounts, according to security researchers at Pen Test Partners LLP, the BBC reports. The watch was designed to feed location and other data to a mobile app on parents' smartphones as well as to let parents listen in on what the children are doing. Pen Test demonstrated how outsiders could track children in real-time and send message to a wearer that look like they are from parents. MiSafes didn't respond to requests for comment from BBC and Pen Test Partners.

Cybersecurity official doesn't use computers. Yoshitaka Sakurada, head of Japan's ruling party, the country's top cybersecurity official and man in charge of the 2020 Tokyo Olympics, said during a legislative meeting Wednesday that he doesn't use a computer, according to an ABC News report Thursday. "I give instructions to my aide," he said. That's one way to keep secure.

News
Case studies Intelligent contracting

925 words
15 November 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Main
18,19
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

WINNER

VMware, Apttus, QuisLex and ThinkSmart (Mitratech)

Standardising contracts

As the contracts essential to VMware's global technology service business increased in both number and in complexity, the company's legal team rewrote its entire contracting process.

In 2013, the team first reduced their involvement in contracting with the help of contract management software from Apttus. This halved the number of contract templates while managing the risks and liabilities in the complex agreements. VMware automated simple contracts such as non-disclosure agreements with the help of QuisLex, the legal process outsourcer. It used ThinkSmart's portal to enable contract approval on "any device, anywhere".

Finalising contracts now happens 50 per cent faster and the work with QuisLex saves more than $8.4m a year.

Axa

Smart contracts and blockchain

Thousands of EU residents experience flight delays of more than two hours every year, but only a few claim compensation. The insurance group Axa is making the process' easier with Fizzy, a smart contract launched in 2017 that uses blockchain to automate compensation.

Customers enter their flight details and the application uses data from the previous seven years to show the risk of delay and quotes for coverage. If a flight is delayed for more than two hours, the smart contractself-executes and compensates the policyholder. It currently covers 70 per cent of EU flights.

Axiom and the University of North Carolina

Standardising contracts

When the University of North Carolina set out to reduce the time spent negotiating clinical trial and confidentiality agreements, it looked at where negotiations had failed previously. With the help of Axiom, the managed services provider, it was able to identify contractual clauses that caused repeated problems. As a result, negotiation times have dropped by up to 70 per cent since April, generating revenues of more than $30m a year.

Capgemini

Smart contracts and blockchain

Professional services company Capgemini operates in 40 countries, employs more than 200,000 people and works with contracts in multiple languages. The range and diversity of its contracts had grown each year, until it exceeded company capacity. Lawyers used Symfact to collate and digitise more than 10,000 contracts, storing them in a searchable central repository.

Next, iManage Ravn was used to review high-value portfolios. The technology tracks obligations, flags breaches and identifies renewals. Visualisation tool Tableau was then used to create digestible reports, summarising entire client portfolios.

Dropbox and Ironclad

Automation

The Dropbox legal team wanted to reduce the time spent reviewing simple legal contracts and turned to Ironclad, a US West Coast-based provider of contract management software. Together, they created a playbook for nondisclosure agreements that non-specialist business colleagues could execute. Almost a quarter of the company's estimated 6,000 annual contracts have been executed on Ironclad since 2016.

The software integrates with other technology and is easy to adopt, which allowed the lawyers at Dropbox to focus on more complex transactions.

eBay and LawGeex

Simplifying the approvals process

Working with Israeli artificial intelligence company LawGeex, eBay streamlined the way it handled its high volume of "low-risk, lowdollar" contracts. When simple contracts reach the management system, the AI reviews them and assigns them a risk level. Low-risk "green" documents receive a brief human review before being sent for immediate signature. If it identifies a more significant risk, the contract is escalated for review. This triage approach reduced review times by up to 50 per cent in some cases in the past year.

HPE and Apttus

Reducing lawyer involvement

The enterprise information technology company HPE implemented Apttus's contract management tool to store contracts, manage and automate parts of the process, and collect new data. This cut contract response times in half from 48 to 24 hours, and halved negotiation times. The tool has also collected more than 3m data points, which are used to identify risk, predicting contracting costs with 99 per cent accuracy.

London Stock Exchange Group, Radiant Law, Lawyers on Demand

Standardising contracts

Facing an increased need for legal services, the London Stock Exchange Group first standardised contract templates and automated its processes in 2011 with the help of Radiant Law, a technology and outsourcing boutique.

Early this year it enlisted flexible resourcing firm Lawyers on Demand to help with procurement and intragroup agreements. The dual-provider model also trials technologies for the London Stock Exchange, allowing it to bring products to market faster.

Shell International

Visual contracts

When the legal team at Shell decided to make its contracting process easier for non-English speaking customers, they designed a visual contract, which was piloted this year. Starting with Shell's marine lubricants business, the legal team rewrote its general terms and conditions in plain English, added clear headings and visual elements and reduced the word count by 38 per cent. It has led to a significantly faster renewal of hard-to-close accounts since the change.

The AA and Thomson Reuters Legal Managed Services, Syke, Thomson Reuters Contract Express, eBrevia

Integrating different technologies

With just eight weeks to review and amend 2,600 contracts before the introduction of the EU's GDPR (General Data Protection Regulation), the AA engaged consulting group Syke. Thomson Reuters then used eBrevia's artificial intelligence tool to review and extract contract data from the AA's agreements and automatically generate new contracts with Contract Express software.

The automated process allowed the AA to meet the deadline at a fifth of the cost of a traditional approach, and agreement data were added to the AA's SAP systems.

Vestar Capital Partners is investing in Information Resources Inc., a provider of data and predictive analytics backed by New Mountain Capital.

New Mountain and Vestar in a press release said they would jointly govern Information Resources once the transaction closes and that New Mountain and other existing investors will retain a "meaningful investment" in the company. Terms of the agreement, which is expected to close in the fourth quarter, weren't disclosed.

Based in Chicago, Information Resources offers big data, predictive analytics and insights to consumer packaged goods, over-the-counter health care, retail, financial services and media companies. The company offers an on-demand, cloud-based technology platform, which has a repository of purchase, media, social, causal and loyalty data, to its more than 5,000 clients around the world.

New Mountain acquired Information Resources in 2011. The firm said that during its ownership, Information Resources made "significant investments in leading-edge technology and built a partner network of more than 100 companies."

Jefferies Financial Group Inc., Nomura Securities Co. and Ares Management LP provided committed financing for the transaction. Kirkland & Ellis LLP served as legal counsel to Vestar. Evercore Inc., Morgan Stanley and Jefferies acted as financial adviser and Fried Frank Harris Shriver & Jacobson LLP served as legal counsel to New Mountain and Information Resources.

New Mountain currently manages private-equity, public-equity and credit funds with more than $20 billion in assets under management. The New York firm last year "https://www.wsj.com/articles/new-mountain-raises-6-15-billion-for-fund-v-1506624998?mod=searchresults&page=4&pos=1&mod=article_inline" its fifth private-equity fund with $6.15 billion.

Today's Top Stories

Robotic bidding: Companies vying for supremacy in robotic software are sparking an investment frenzy. In the latest escalation of the bidding, SoftBank Group Corp. has thrown its weight behind Automation Anywhere Inc. for a fresh investment that values the Silicon Valley startup at $2.6 billion. This comes in addition to Automation Anywhere's $250 million fundraising in July. And it follows the $3 billion valuation that competitor UiPath Inc. recently received. WSJ's Katie Roof and Rolfe Winkler have more.

Motley LPs: Motley Fool Ventures hopes its debut venture fund will best other attempts at democratizing venture capital. The fund, launched by privately held Motley Fool Holdings, has collected $145 million from some 800 individual limited partners. It's an exceptionally large haul for a traditionally structured venture fund raised entirely from individual investors. WSJ Pro's Yuliya Chernova has the exclusive.

Grab and go: With the advent of automated cashier technology pioneered by Amazon.com and others, consumers are learning how to shop and exit stores without being rung up at a traditional checker at a cash register. One autonomous-checkout startup, Standard Cognition, has raised $40 million in Series A funding led by Initialized Capital, with participation by CRV, Y Combinator and Draper Associates. The startup's technology uses in-store cameras to identify what shoppers pick up and then charges their credit card when they leave. WSJ's Tomio Geron has the story.

Mack's Take:  Death by Pilot' Isn't Only Hazard for Health Tech

By Heather Mack

It's harder to succeed in health care than in pure technology, as every startup is painfully aware.

But the latest reminder came with the abrupt flameout of Driver, a clinical trial matching platform that had the ambitious goal of expanding cancer treatment options around the world. Despite a prestigious lineup of partnerships and $100 million in backing, Driver ran out of money and shut down operations just a few weeks after launching.

The reasons for its demise aren't complex: Consumers don't want to pay for health tech tools, and a company can go broke in the process of trying to prove its worth.

"Death by Pilot" is a common refrain in the industry. The startup burns through capital running collaborations with hospitals before it is able to generate enough revenue. Indeed, people weren't buying Driver's $3,000 app, and partners including the Mayo Clinic didn't bring the startup revenue it needed.

What's even riskier than pilots, some investors said, is running a startup with an unclear business model.

Industry News

A tidal wave of cash has helped turn China's internet startups into formidable superpowers rivaling their U.S. counterparts. But venture capitalists argue that a unique set of forces are also spurring the startups' ascendancy. Behind China's rise is a mobile-first mind-set among entrepreneurs who preach meritocracy, have easy access to centralized personal data and are adept at so-called growth hacks to aggregate supply and demand quickly, said venture capitalists at the WSJ Tech D.Live conference on Wednesday. Read more.

Uber Technologies Inc. said third-quarter revenue rose 38% from a year earlier to $2.95 billion, but that was less than the second-quarter year-over-year jump of 63%. Its loss widened to $1.07 billion from $891 million in the second quarter. Read more.

Dragonfly Therapeutics Inc. secured $50 million through a new cancer drug-development deal with biopharmaceutical company Celgene Corp., extending a collaboration the companies formed last year. Last year, Dragonfly pulled in $33 million through a deal with Celgene to develop immuno-oncology drugs that take aim at blood cancer. The alliance now has expanded its mission to include treatments for solid tumors. Read more.

Vox Media is creating "A-List," a channel within its Concert advertising network that enables marketers to buy ads across several entertainment-focused brands including websites from New York Media, such as Vulture, New York Magazine and The Cut, as well as Rolling Stone, Vox and E!, CMO Today reported.

News
Experian: settling scores

328 words
14 November 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
10
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Twitter: @FTLex

Data is proliferating. Experian is expanding with it. Despite regulatory concerns the UK information services group looks set for further growth.

Companies can sell more, with less risk, if they can accurately score a potential customer's creditworthiness. Providing the data and products that allow them quickly to make such decisions makes up more than half of Experian's revenue (the rest comes from software, analytics and consumer services). All told, these services helped deliver group organic revenue growth of a chunky 8 per cent for the six months to the end of September.

Experian said yesterday that it expected that pace of sales growth to continue for the full year. That explains why the company's shares trade on a forward price/earnings multiple of 24 times   a small but deserved premium to a basket of peers.

Experian's services are awkward to replace, once embedded with clients. The company has a big exposure to developed economies such as the US, which accounts for two-fifths of group revenues. There, the business servicing the healthcare sector saw revenues grow by double digits.

The stock's rating also reflects opportunities in emerging markets. Asia-Pacific is of particular interest. Indonesia, for example, has tens of millions of consumers cut off from the formal banking system. But these people have mobile phones, from which a credit profile can be derived.

China is not an option for expansion, as Beijing balks at foreign companies using citizens' data. Other jurisdictions are getting more restrictive too.

Experian is among a number of companies accused by Privacy International of breaching GDPR, the EU's new data protection law. The company denies doing so.

Of greater concern is a big hack like that suffered by Equifax, a US-based rival. Yet even Equifax is still increasing its sales, a year after one of the worst breaches in history. That is a measure of how solid this industry is.

The Roundup
