U.S. & CANADA

BUYOUTS

Aldrich Capital Partners, Vienna, Va.

Growth-equity firm Aldrich Capital Partners closed its debut fund with $256 million, said firm co-founders Mirza Baig and Raheel Zia. Aldrich Capital Partners Fund LP had a $250 million goal, according to August filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The fund's backers include the Connecticut Retirement Plans and Trust Funds, the New York State Common Retirement Fund and the California Public Employees' Retirement System, according to pension documents. The firm will use the capital to invest in North American companies in the lower middle market, focusing on three technology subsectors: health-care technology, financial technology and application software.

Argosy Partners, Toronto

Argosy Partners said it will raise Shotgun Fund IV, a vehicle focused on "shotgun" deals in which one business partner wants to buy out the other. The firm is seeking up to 50 million Canadian dollars ($38.3 million) for the fund. Since its inception in 1999, the Shotgun Fund series has provided an "equity solution for the owner-managers of private, small and medium-sized Canadian businesses involved in shareholder disputes, or who simply require shareholder liquidity quickly and quietly," the firm said in a news release.

Arrowroot Capital Management, Santa Monica, Calif.

Software investor Arrowroot Capital Management is aiming to raise $315 million for its fourth growth-equity fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The goal for Arrowroot Capital IV LP is about 78% higher than the amount Arrowroot raised for its third fund, which closed last year with more than $177 million in limited-partner and general-partner commitments. The firm backs business-to-business software companies, aiming to invest $5 million to $25 million and taking either minority or majority ownership stakes.

C ordillera Investment Partners, San Francisco

Cordillera Investment Partners closed its second fund with $362 million. The amount collected for Cordillera Investment Fund II LP brings the firm's assets under management to $648 million. Cordillera in a news release said it targets investments it considers "unique and less correlated &in areas that it believes remain inefficient, misunderstood and not overcapitalized." The firm has invested in areas such as music royalties, water rights, international arbitration, commercial litigation finance and broadband spectrum licenses. Cordillera's previous fund closed on $197 million in 2017, according to a May document from the Utah School & Institutional Trust Funds Office, which states the latest fund had a $350 million target.

Greenbriar Equity Group, Rye, N.Y.

Transportation investor Greenbriar Equity Group is more than halfway to the fundraising goal for its latest vehicle, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Greenbriar Equity Fund IV LP and a parallel fund have gathered at least $607.8 million so far toward a $1 billion goal, the filings show. The firm invests in transportation and related industries, focusing on manufacturers and companies in the aerospace, automotive, distribution and logistics sectors, among others. Greenbriar's previous fund closed on $1.1 billion in 2014.

HealthpointCapital, New York

HealthpointCapital, a private-equity firm focused on the musculoskeletal sector, held a first closing on $100 million for its fourth fund. Musculoskeletal Fund IV has a $500 million target. HealthpointCapital said the new vehicle will invest in eight to 10 growth-stage technology and services companies in the orthopedic, spine, dental, maxillofacial, rehabilitation and digital-health sectors. The firm collected at least $410.8 million for HealthpointCapital Partners III LP, which was seeking $700 million, according to a 2008 regulatory filing. It isn't clear how much HealthpointCapital ultimately collected for the vehicle. The firm closed HealthpointCapital Partners II LP with $420 million in 2007.

H.I.G. Capital, Miami

H.I.G. Capital's growth-capital unit closed its third fund with $970 million of commitments. WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported H.I.G. Growth Buyouts & Equity Fund III LP was nearing a $950 million goal listed in a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Backers of the new fund include the Texas County & District Retirement System and Maine Public Employees Retirement System. H.I.G.'s latest vehicle will continue a strategy of investing in growth buyouts and structured equity in small-cap companies primarily in North America. The firm's previous fund in the series, H.I.G. Growth Equity Fund II LP, closed with $500 million in 2011. The firm also has collected at least $1.12 billion for a new offering the firm plans to commit to other H.I.G.-sponsored funds, according to another SEC filing. The amount for H.I.G. Strategic Partners Fund LP surpasses a $1 billion target indicated in an investor presentation made to the Texas Municipal Retirement System. It isn't known from the SEC filing whether the fund has officially closed. According to the presentation, H.I.G. plans to commit the vehicle to 12 funds the firm expects to bring to market over the next four years, as well as to selective co-investments alongside those funds.

Level Equity Management, New York

Level Equity Management closed Level Equity Growth Partners IV LP and Level Equity Opportunities Fund 2018 LP, securing $535 million and $200 million, respectively, in limited-partner commitments. Founded in 2009, Level has 26 team members and invests in software and technology-driven businesses. In July, the firm said it was making a growth-stage investment in TeamSupport LLC, a provider of customer support software to the business-to-business market.

LFM Capital, Nashville, Tenn.

LFM Capital closed its second fund, LFM Capital Partners II LP, with $184 million of commitments. The new fund received support from investors in the firm's first fund a $100 million vehicle and new backers. LFM acquires U.S. manufacturing and industrial-services companies with at least $3 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization and enterprise values of $15 million to $125 million.

MidOcean Partners, New York

MidOcean Partners wrapped up its latest private-equity fund with $1.2 billion of investor commitments. The final closing of MidOcean Partners V LP came in at the fund's hard cap and ahead of its $1 billion target. The fundraising effort was the firm's most ambitious one since it closed its third fund above its $1.25 billion hard cap in 2007. The firm's next fund, MidOcean Partners IV LP, was a 2015 bridge fund that collected $300 million, a person familiar with the matter told WSJ Pro Private Equity last year. MidOcean targets control investments with minimum equity contribution of $25 million.

Piper Jaffray Merchant Banking, Minneapolis

Piper Jaffray Merchant Banking, a private-equity arm of Piper Jaffray Cos., raised $130 million for its second growth- equity fund. The final closing of Piper Jaffray Merchant Banking Fund II LP brings the group's total assets to $275 million across three funds. Piper Jaffray, which plans to write equity checks of up to $15 million per deal, typically targets late-stage investments in companies with at least $10 million of 12-months-trailing revenue.

Rothschild & Co., Paris

The private-equity arm of European banking group Rothschild & Co., closed its first U.S. buyout fund, Five Arrows Capital Partners LP, at more than $655 million. WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported the Rothschild arm, Five Arrows Capital Partners, had set up a new office in New York and was seeking $500 million to invest in midmarket companies. Five Arrows will focus on deals with lower midmarket North American businesses valued at $75 million to $500 million in the health-care, data, software, and business-services sectors. The Rothschild unit targets equity investments of $30 million to $70 million, with the ability to commit larger amounts with the fund's limited partners.

Tailwind Capital, New York

Tailwind Capital closed its latest fund with $1.8 billion to invest in the health-care, business-services and industrial- services sectors. The firm is deploying capital from Tailwind Capital Partners III LP in North American businesses valued at as much as $250 million. Investors that committed to the fund include the Oregon Public Employees Retirement Fund and the Washington State Investment Board, according to investor documents. Tailwind's previous fund collected $950 million and began investing in 2013, WSJ Pro Private Equity previously reported, citing an investor presentation.

Teall Capital Partners, Winston-Salem, N.C.

Teall Capital Partners closed its debut fund, raising more than $68 million in committed capital. The firm aimed to gather $80 million for Teall Growth Fund I LP, according to a May filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The lifestyle brands and services firm was formed in 2017 by Ben C. Sutton Jr., the former chairman of college sports business IMG College and the founder, chairman and chief executive of ISP Sports.

Trinity Hunt Partners, Dallas

Trinity Hunt Partners closed its fifth fund at the vehicle's $350 million hard cap. Trinity Hunt Partners V LP exceeded an initial target of $300 million. The firm's previous fund closed on $211 million in 2013. Trinity Hunt targets founder- and family-owned companies in the business-services, industrial, consumer and health-care sectors. The firm invests in companies with $5 million to $25 million in earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

Vista Equity Partners, Austin, Texas

Vista Equity Partners has collected $11.4 billion so far for its latest fund, and it isn't done yet. The private-equity firm is well on its way to raising a record-breaking $16 billion fund that will buy software companies, said people familiar with the fundraising. Vista held an initial closing for the flagship fund in late September, said a person familiar with the situation. If Vista Equity Partners Fund VII LP hits its goal, it would be the largest such buyout fund raised by an independent private- equity firm. It also would be Vista's largest fund to date. Vista wrapped up its previous fund last year with nearly $11.1 billion.

FUNDS OF FUNDS

Adams Street Partners, Chicago

Adams Street Partners raised $426 million for a fund-of-funds vehicle that will back early-stage venture-capital funds. Adams Street Venture Innovation Fund II LP's predecessor collected at least $202.5 million as of January 2017, according to a regulatory filing. Adams Street closed on $475 million in 2014 to make direct investments in venture- and growth-stage businesses.

MEZZANINE & OTHER DEBT

Balance Point Capital, Westport, Conn.

Balance Point Capital closed its latest midmarket fund at $380 million. The final tally for Balance Point Capital Partners III LP came in ahead of the fund's $300 million target. The firm plans to use the new fund to originate and underwrite debt and equity investments, including first- and second-lien debt, unitranche debt, subordinated debt and equity. Balance Point typically invests $10 million to $50 million in companies with $10 million to $150 million of revenue and $3 million to $30 million of earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation and amortization.

Cross Ocean Partners, Greenwich, Conn.

Credit investor Cross Ocean Partners closed on more than $1 billion for two new funds. The firm held a final closing for its first aviation fund on about $438 million in committed capital. The vehicle makes investments globally in late-life commercial aircraft and engines, with an emphasis on North America and Europe. Cross Ocean also closed its first U.S. special situations credit fund with total commitments of about $605 million. The special situations strategy invests in stressed and distressed corporate debt, primarily senior and asset-backed instruments.

New Energy Capital Partners, Hanover, N.H.

Renewable-energy investor New Energy Capital Partners is seeking $400 million for its second credit fund. The firm is raising its new fund through two parallel vehicles, New Energy Capital Infrastructure Credit Fund II LP and New Energy Capital Infrastructure Credit Fund II-A LP, according to filings with the Securities and Exchange Commission. New Energy is seeking more capital for its new fund than it did for its maiden infrastructure credit vehicle, which closed last year with $325 million. The firm at the time said that fund would focus on infrastructure assets in North America and invest through different types of securities, including senior debt, subordinated debt and preferred stock. New Energy typically invests in small and midsize clean energy-infrastructure projects with total capital needs of $20 million to $300 million.

Solar Capital Partners, New York

Solar Capital Partners closed on more than $750 million in equity commitments for its latest private-credit funds. SCP Private Credit Income Fund LP and SCP Private Credit Income BDC LLC will invest in U.S. midmarket companies through directly originated senior secured cash flow loans, asset-based loans and life science loans. The $750 million-plus of committed capital came from access funds comprised of capital from Goldman Sachs Group Inc.'s private-wealth- management clients. The funds will have about $1.9 billion of investable capital, including anticipated leverage.

SECONDARY

Commonfund, Wilton, Conn.

Commonfund has raised at least $243 million so far for its latest secondary fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The amount recorded for Commonfund Capital Secondary Partners II LP puts the vehicle more than two-thirds of the way toward a $350 million goal indicated in the filing. The amount also surpasses the roughly $170 million the firm raised for its previous secondary fund. Commonfund focuses its secondary strategy on smaller deals, which the firm says tend to be less heavily intermediated.

Landmark Partners, Simsbury, Conn.

Landmark Partners held a $7 billion final closing for its latest secondary private-equity pool, beating an initial $4 billion target. Landmark Equity Partners XVI LP and secondary co-investment affiliates raised more than double the amount gathered by the firm's previous fund in the series, which closed on $3.25 billion in 2015. Backers of Fund XVI include the New York State Common Retirement Fund and the Maryland State Retirement and Pension System, according to pension fund documents. Landmark specializes in secondary deals for stakes in private-equity, real-estate and infrastructure funds.

Northleaf Capital Partners, Toronto

Northleaf Capital Partners gathered $2.2 billion across two new funds and a number of separate accounts. The firm closed Northleaf Secondary Partners II with $800 million, also gathering commitments for Northleaf Private Equity Investors VII and several separately managed accounts. Northleaf didn't state how much the latter fund or the accounts collected. Northleaf Private Equity Investors VII will be open to new investors through early 2019, according to the firm. That fund and the secondary vehicle have begun their investment periods, which will continue through 2020. The secondary fund exceeded its target and the $255 million collected by its predecessor, which closed in 2014. The firm's previous Northleaf Private Equity Investors fund launched in 2013, according to the firm's website, which doesn't state how much the vehicle gathered.

VENTURE CAPITAL

5AM Ventures, San Francisco

Slightly more than two years after closing its most recent fund, life science investor 5AM Ventures is seeking $350 million for a new vehicle, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. If it reaches its goal, 5AM Ventures VI LP would gather more than the $285 million collected by the firm's previous fund, which wrapped up in 2016. 5AM has seen a number of its early bets go public recently. Those companies include cancer drugmaker Arvinas Inc. and gene-therapy and gene-editing startup Homology Medicines Inc. 5AM also has recently backed new companies such as Akouos Inc., a developer of gene therapies for hearing loss.

Amplify Partners, Menlo Park, Calif.

Early-stage investor Amplify Partners raised $200 million for its third fund. The vehicle, Amplify Partners III LP, surpassed the size of its predecessor, which closed at $125 million in 2015. Amplify aims to invest in startups that use deep technologies such as machine learning.

Atomic Management, San Francisco

Atomic Management, a venture firm that exclusively backs companies it develops in-house, has raised $150 million for its second fund. Founded in 2013 and backed by investors that include Peter Thiel and Marc Andreessen, the startup studio launched 10 companies out of its $20 million first fund. It plans to form about 25 startups with the second, said co-founder and Managing Partner Jack Abraham. Atomic spends about $100,000 building prototypes, then will commit $2 million to $3 million to develop a team and product for the best companies.

Benchmark, Woodside, Calif.

Benchmark an early investor in Uber Technologies Inc., Snap Inc. and WeWork Cos. is seeking $425 million for Benchmark Capital Partners IX LP, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. Benchmark is seeking the same amount it recorded for its previous fund in a 2013 SEC filing.

Chrysalix Venture Capital, Vancouver, British Columbia

Chrysalix Venture Capital has closed on almost half of its $120 million target for a new fund called Chrysalix RoboValley Fund, according to a representative for the Canadian firm, which closed its last fund in 2010. Chrysalix's new fund will invest in technologies that improve the efficiency of industries such as energy, chemicals, mobility, materials and mining.

Edison Partners, Princeton, N.J.

Edison Partners closed its ninth and largest growth-equity fund to invest in financial-technology, health-care technology and enterprise-software companies. The vehicle, Edison Partners IX LP, collected $365 million, exceeding its $300 million target as well as its hard cap. The sum also surpassed the $276 million collected by the fund's predecessor, which closed in 2016. Limited partners that back Fund IX include the New Mexico Educational Retirement Board, Rutgers University and the Renaissance Venture Capital Fund. Edison Partners invests in companies with $10 million to $20 million in revenue and annual revenue growth rates of 30% to 50%, said Managing Partner Chris Sugden.

Evolv Ventures, Chicago

Kraft Heinz Co., the giant behind foods such as Oscar Mayer hot dogs and Mac & Cheese, has launched a venture-capital fund to invest in a broad range of food-focused startups. With $100 million for the first fund, Evolv Ventures will back companies at multiple points in the food chain. The fund will invest in Series A through C rounds and write checks averaging $4 million to $5 million, said Evolv Ventures General Partner Bill Pescatello.

First Round Capital, Philadelphia

Early-stage venture firm First Round Capital is raising a new fund that is seeking about $209 million, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. The firm founded by Josh Kopelman has backed hundreds of startups. The new fund, First Round Capital VII LP, is to be slightly larger than its predecessor, which closed with $175 million in 2016. The firm also is raising a side fund seeking $10.6 million, a separate filing shows.

Humbition, New York

Two experienced New York entrepreneurs are launching a fund to invest in their hometown. Backed by investors that include Founders Fund and Atomico, the new firm, called humbition, has raised $30 million for its first fund. The firm's founders are Cyrus Massoumi, ZocDoc Inc. co-founder and former chief executive officer; and Slava Rubin, Indiegogo Inc. co-founder and former CEO. Humbition expects to write checks of $2 million to $5 million for New York-based early-stage startups.

Lytical Ventures, New York

Investment management firm Lyrical Partners is backing a new venture-capital firm, Lytical Ventures, which will make early-stage investments in cybersecurity, big data and artificial intelligence. Lytical Ventures has closed on $20 million for its first fund, with an anchor investment by Lyrical Partners. The fund could reach $60 million, according to the firm. Lytical Ventures is led by Lucas Nelson and Steve Berg, who previously worked at Evolution Equity and Antecedent Ventures, respectively.

New Enterprise Associates, Menlo Park, Calif.

New Enterprise Associates, one of the nation's oldest and largest venture firms, has raised at least half of the capital planned for its new spinout, said people familiar with the situation. The firm, to be called NewView, is expected to hold secondary shares in late-stage private companies, including consumer genomics company 23andMe Inc. and education startup Duolingo Inc. NewView aims to raise about $1.37 billion, said one of the people. Another person with knowledge of the fundraising in September told WSJ Pro Private Equity the fund had secured about $700 million. Goldman Sachs Group Inc. is a lead investor as part of a larger group putting money into the deal, said one of the people and a third person familiar with the situation. The group also includes investment management firm Hamilton Lane, one of the people said. About $1 billion of the NewView fund will be devoted to buying the equity stakes from NEA, said one of the people. The rest of the fund, about $370 million, will be reserved for follow-on investments in the existing portfolio.

RiverVest Venture Partners, St. Louis

Life science investor RiverVest Venture Partners has raised at least $167.5 million for a new fund, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission. RiverVest invests in biotechnology and medical-technology startups and has scored several recent exits, including the sale this year of medtech startup Securus Medical Group to Boston Scientific Corp. The amount recorded for RiverVest Venture Fund IV LP exceeds a $150 million goal recorded in a previous filing. RiverVest closed its previous fund in the series at $80.2 million in 2015. RiverVest's strategy includes founding and incubating startups and investing in some later-stage companies.

WeWork Cos., New York

WeWork Cos. is launching a venture fund to invest in "the future of work" and other startups, said people familiar with the plans. Emily Keeton, who joined WeWork last year as global head of mergers and acquisitions, is now managing director at WeWork Creator Fund. Ms. Keeton is planning to assemble a team of about five people behind the effort, said a person familiar with the situation. The size of WeWork's fund couldn't be determined. WeWork has started taking outside capital that will add to the balance-sheet resources it is dedicating to the fund, one of the people said. The fund already has started evaluating startup deals, the people said. WeWork Creator Fund will seek out social-impact startups, as well as those in the future of work category, one person said.

EUROPE

BUYOUTS

Altor Equity Partners, Stockholm

Altor Equity Partners is back in market seeking  2.5 billion ($2.93 billion) for its latest flagship fund, said people familiar with the matter. The fund, which will be the firm's fifth, is expected to be heavily oversubscribed, one of the people said. If the vehicle hits its target, it will be the largest the firm has ever raised. Its predecessor closed on  2 billion in 2014.

Epiris, London

Epiris raised 821 million ($1.06 billion) for its first buyout fund since it split from Electra Private Equity PLC. The fund was backed by institutional and private investors mostly from North America and the U.K. WSJ Pro Private Equity reported in November 2017 that Epiris was seeking to raise 1 billion after kicking off its first institutional fundraising since it unveiled plans to separate from listed investment trust Electra Private Equity in late 2016. The firm held a first closing for the fund on 500 million earlier this year.

Inverleith, Edinburgh

Inverleith held a  60 million ($69.7 million) final closing for its debut private-equity fund. The fund will invest in consumer businesses in the U.K. that are valued at as much as  30 million. Inverleith was set up in 2010 by former investment banker Ben Thomson, who now is chairman and partner of the firm.

Keensight Capital, Paris

Keensight Capital is in talks with investors about raising its fifth private-equity fund, said a person familiar with the matter. The fund is seeking  750 million ($877 million), substantially more than the  450 million the firm raised for its previous pool of capital, Keensight IV. Keensight, which spun out of the Rothschild & Co. banking group in 2013, invests in companies throughout Europe with revenue of  15 million to  250 million. The firm focuses on deals in the internet, media, information-technology, health-care and services sectors.

SECONDARY

Balderton Capital, London

Balderton Capital closed a $145 million secondary fund to purchase stakes in European technology companies from early shareholders. The fund, Balderton Liquidity I, will invest in growth-stage companies where early investors and employees are eager for liquidity ahead of an exit through a public offering or trade sale. The fund will seek investments in sectors similar to where Balderton invests its existing early-stage fund. That fund primarily backs software and internet-powered companies in Europe. The firm's previous fund closed at $375 million in 2017.

ASIA

BUYOUTS

Hillhouse Capital Group, Hong Kong

Hillhouse Capital Group, a Chinese investment firm that has backed many prominent internet and consumer companies, closed a new $10.6 billion fund in what is the largest-ever capital raise by a private-equity firm in Asia. The fund's size tops a $9.3 billion Asia private-equity fund set up last year by KKR & Co. Hillhouse said the new fund will look to invest in companies in the health-care, consumer, technology and service sectors globally, with a focus on Asia.

VENTURE CAPITAL

Dragonfly Capital Partners, San Francisco

New firm Dragonfly Capital Partners has raised $100 million to invest in crypto funds and seed-stage startups. More than half of Dragonfly's fund will be deployed in crypto vehicles. The firm, which invests both in Asia and the U.S., is led by Alexander Pack, a former investor at Bain Capital Ventures, and Bo Feng, a founding partner of Ceyuan Ventures in China.

GGV Capital, Menlo Park, Calif.

GGV Capital, an investor in the U.S. and China, has raised $1.88 billion across four new investment vehicles. The firm, which has backed companies such as Alibaba Group Holding Ltd. and Square Inc., said it would use the capital to invest in startups at all stages. A pair of main funds, GGV Capital VII LP and GGV Capital VII Plus LP, totaled $1.36 billion and will be invested in late-stage and follow-on opportunities. GGV Discovery II LP raised $460 million for seed and early-stage investments. GGV also let the founders of its portfolio companies invest as limited partners in its $60 million GGV Capital VII Entrepreneurs Fund LP.

LATIN AMERICA

BUYOUTS

Oria Capital, So Paulo

Oria Capital is seeking $100 million for its third fund, as the private-equity firm looks to capitalize on the growth of Brazil's technology market, according to people familiar with the matter. Fund-of-funds manager Spectra Investments is likely to invest up to 50 million Brazilian reais ($12.2 million) in Oria Tech III, said one of the people. Oria originally was the private-equity arm of asset manager DLM Invista, from which it spun off in 2015 under the name DLM Private Equity. The firm rebranded as Oria in 2016. Its first fund closed on BRL170 million in 2012, before the firm spun off from DLM, one of the people said. In 2018, Oria raised BRL71 million for a second pool of capital, which was used to acquire the portfolio of Intel Capital in Brazil. Oria focuses on technology-based companies operating in the business-to- business services sector, particularly providers of software-as-a-service products.

The Take: Finding a Universal Language for Investment Impact

By Luis Garcia

Private investors have long struggled to find a common language to define, let alone measure, the social or environmental impact of their investments.

Now, some are beginning to believe the United Nations' sustainable development goals may provide that language.

The SDGs are a set of 17 goals that address the world's most pressing development challenges. Agreed upon by 193 U.N. member states in 2015, the global goals as the SDGs also are called include eliminating poverty and hunger, achieving gender equality, protecting the environment, promoting sustainable economic growth and making sure people can live healthy lives. Read Garcia's Take.

Bain Capital Ventures Raises $1 Billion for New Funds; After backing startups like DocuSign, Jet.com and SendGrid, the firm is the latest to raise $1 billion

By Tomio Geron 
350 words
14 November 2018
12:30
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Bain Capital Ventures, which has invested in DocuSign, Jet.com, and SendGrid, has raised $1 billion for its latest set of funds as venture firms mount ever-larger war chests for backing startups.

The funds include $650 million for its early-stage fund, $250 million for a co-investment fund for growth stage deals, plus $100 million in separate vehicles to invest on behalf of its partners. That's an increase from the firm's "https://www.wsj.com/articles/bain-capital-ventures-closes-600m-fund-1467993136" to raise a $600 million early-stage fund and a $200 million growth fund.

Areas like artificial intelligence, cloud and autonomous vehicles are driving Bain's interest in deals across a number of sectors. The firm invests about 80% of its funds in business-to-business startups, with the rest in consumer or emerging technology. Its areas of focus include software as a service, cybersecurity and e-commerce.

About half of Bain's capital, now with $4.9 billion under management, is invested in early-stage deals. Last year Boston-based Bain launched a program to invest in a network of angel investors its version of a scout program. That has led to Bain doing Series A deals in crypto startup "https://www.wsj.com/articles/basis-raises-133-million-to-create-tokens-with-price-stability-1524097063" and cybersecurity startup Red Balloon Security Inc., along with 12 seed investments such as "https://www.wsj.com/articles/money-markets-for-crypto-assets-are-in-the-works-1526470201" and Lumina.

Ajay Agarwal, managing director, said Bain is optimistic about trends in enterprise with the acceleration of cloud computing, disruption of traditional industries and more mergers and acquisitions driven by incumbents buying cloud companies.

"We've had such a long history of success in b-to-b and so many assets we bring to the table for early-stage founders," Mr. Agarwal said. Bain will keep investing in consumer startups, adding to previous deals such as Jet.com and Rent the Runway. Other past investments include LinkedIn, Kiva Systems and SurveyMonkey.

Today's Top Stories

Geron's Take: Can Shopper Rewards Help Bitcoin Go Mainstream?

By Tomio Geron

Few real-world applications for bitcoin have broken into the mainstream.

But a new startup is betting it has found a way to make the virtual currency at once relevant and accessible via something people do every day: shopping.

The startup is called Lolli and it runs a rewards program that offers consumers bitcoin payments for shopping online. Although similar to cash-back-reward credit cards, Lolli's payout is exclusively in bitcoin and can be in sums larger than the 1% to 2% of cash purchases offered by standard card loyalty programs.

More in Geron's Take.

At Palantir, a Unicorn Tries to Grow Up --- The hot startup is under pressure to curb perks, boost revenue ahead of a possible IPO

By Rob Copeland and Eliot Brown 
2,164 words
13 November 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

PALO ALTO, Calif. -- Palantir Technologies Inc. appears to be a prosperous Silicon Valley spy-software machine. It crunches masses of data to find insights. Its analytics have been credited with helping track down Osama bin Laden. Its value by one measure is $20 billion, one of the highest among private tech companies, and its founders include famed investor Peter Thiel.

Behind this hot startup image, Palantir has struggled to live up to its reputation.

The company has begun planning an initial public offering but hasn't turned an annual profit in its 14-year history. It is trying to win more corporate customers, after years of flailing attempts, although some potential clients view Palantir as flippant toward business and are wary of its work for governments and intelligence agencies. It missed sales targets last year after hoped-for contracts fell through.

Its engineers were accustomed to spending profligately -- the company sponsored 13-course tasting-menu lunches with lobster tail and sashimi at headquarters -- calling such extravagance "Palantir Entitlement Syndrome."

Alex Karp, the company's chief executive, has added to the unconventional atmosphere. When he grows excited about an idea, say current and former employees, he balls his fists and taps employees rapid-fire in the solar plexus.

This all leaves Palantir, and Mr. Karp, at a familiar juncture in Silicon Valley. It has run like a scrappy startup even though it has become a global company. Facing appeals from investors and employees to go public, so they can more easily sell shares, Mr. Karp must now make Palantir into a more traditional operation.

"We're a company that's going into adolescence," Mr. Karp, 51 years old, says. "The rules for being a child and the rules for being an adult are different."

Palantir is among a host of "unicorns" -- startups valued over $1 billion -- that are losing money years after inception. Uber Technologies Inc. and WeWork Cos. have burned through more than $8 billion and $2 billion, respectively, and still lose money. That's in contrast to an earlier generation. Facebook Inc. turned an annual profit after five years.

Some investors are complaining to Palantir executives about long-delayed IPO plans, say people familiar with their complaints. Its principal patron, Mr. Thiel, pared his ownership in private sales at well below Palantir's $20 billion valuation, which came from its latest funding round, in 2015.

Investors say Mr. Karp and others at Palantir have told them the firm will turn profitable in 2019. Mr. Karp made that promise in some past years, too. And he still sends mixed messages on whether making money matters, often saying his primary focus is building the most important company in the world.

"The unintended consequence of the numbers we are likely to post," he says, "is profitability."

Palantir is interviewing investment banks about plans to go public as soon as next year. Behind the scenes, Mr. Karp is on a sort of apology tour. Meeting this year with clients in Washington, D.C., he pledged to change the impression of the firm's staff as overconfident, condescending and even sloppy.

"We were young kids rocking up in shorts and T-shirts," said Mr. Karp, according to people present, "and we realized that was hurting our credibility."

To cut costs, Palantir has been unloading office properties and slowed expensive hires. It is scrutinizing employee perks such as last-minute international business-class travel, a shock to a firm that once entertained a companywide debate over whether to restore artisanal bacon to the breakfast menu -- "Bacongate," employees called it. Palantir fired two employees who expensed lingerie and suits, people familiar with the episode say.

Palantir is pushing to make its analytics software more attractive to corporations and has won over companies including Credit Suisse Group AG, Merck KGaA, Fiat Chrysler Automobiles NV, United Continental Holdings Inc. and Airbus SE.

Mr. Thiel, still Palantir's largest shareholder, says he has faith in the company. "You go underwater," Mr. Thiel says, "and the important thing is to come up for air at some point."

Interviews with current and former employees, investors and clients give an inside look at how Palantir is trying to turn around its business.

Named after a crystal ball in "The Lord of the Rings" series, Palantir was started in 2004 by recent Stanford University graduates and bankrolled with $30 million mostly from Mr. Thiel, a PayPal co-founder. The Central Intelligence Agency's venture-capital arm later chipped in.

It has raised around $2.5 billion in 12 rounds over 11 years. Investors include former Hollywood agent Michael Ovitz, Home Depot Inc. co-founder Ken Langone and Rupert Murdoch, executive chairman of News Corp, The Wall Street Journal's parent.

Palantir's initial idea focused on selling a fraud-detection tool, similar to what PayPal uses. Customers would be government agencies looking to spot connections across far-flung data sets such as vehicle registrations or the locations of terrorist camps. Palantir soon counted clients including the U.S. Special Forces, the Federal Bureau of Investigation and international intelligence agencies.

Mr. Karp joined as CEO a few months in. While not a trained programmer, the multilingual Mr. Karp, with a philosophy Ph.D., took enthusiastically to running a global organization, spending around 250 days a year traveling. He sometimes answers his office phone loudly in German, nurturing gossip he is talking to one of his girlfriends in Europe.

"We have a large business in Germany," Mr. Karp says about those accounts. "Most of my German is being used to grow the business."

A self-described socialist whose preferred work uniform is athletic wear and bright beanies, he is a fitness nut who lives in a one-room Bavarian-style farmhouse. He occasionally shoves clients in the chest to demonstrate a Chinese martial-arts move to redirect force. He has three Tai Chi swords visible in his New York office and one in Palo Alto.

From the start, Palantir's work was furtive -- many employees had to apply for security clearances. Employees received five-figure bonus checks if they lived close to company offices.

The company early on focused mainly on government business. Its software has been credited by investors and its staff with helping the U.S. capture bin Laden, disrupt broader terrorist networks and chase swindlers including Bernard Madoff.

Outright talk of commerce was for years considered verboten. Mr. Karp would tell staff, in a nod to Palantir's engineering roots: "We are not going to have any f---ing salespeople."

Mr. Karp says: "If it began or ended with some vulgarity, I'm sure I said it."

As Palantir sought to expand from government into private industry, its business plan involved losing money early in hopes of lucrative assignments later. Jobs were labor-intensive, with software written in a bespoke programming language and taught one-on-one.

Instead of salespeople, Palantir sent so-called forward-deployed engineers to meet potential clients. Some engineers answered skeptical questions from business prospects by suggesting companies simply plug their data into Palantir's software and wait for it to spit out results -- a suggestion some took as aggressive and presumptuous.

Mr. Karp says clients have always controlled their own data.

Palantir's strategy with corporations reached a nadir around three years ago, when companies including JPMorgan Chase & Co. and Hershey Co. dropped multimillion-dollar contracts with it. JPMorgan declined to comment. A Hershey spokesman said "Our project with Palantir was part of a learning journey in building these capabilities to garner insights."

Mr. Karp characterizes those struggles as part of a creative process now yielding results.

"This is not a science," he says. "We are a colony of artists. You cannot go to Basquiat or Monet and say, 'Well, that painting didn't capture the time.'"

To better appeal to corporations, Palantir about two years ago began rolling out a new product, Foundry, that amounts to off-the-rack software that amalgamates and crunches data across an organization.

Unlike prior efforts, in which engineers would tailor software client-by-client, Palantir designed Foundry to be more generic and folded into customers' existing computer networks. The software is typically used by dozens or hundreds of workers, not just software engineers who need special training. Palantir says it can potentially assign smaller teams to each client and reap bigger profits.

Foundry is helping Credit Suisse stitch together accounts across the world for a single client and spot potential money laundering, says the bank's chief compliance officer, Lara Warner. Merck uses it to manage sales data of fertility drugs in China and better forecast demand and inventory, a problem for which Merck early dispatched its own data-science team without success, Chief Digital Officer James Kugler says.

Fiat Chrysler uses the system to pinpoint part recalls. United says it began using Foundry this year to crunch customer surveys on when, for instance, an aircraft seat won't recline, and shoot the information to the appropriate mechanic where the plane has spare time on the ground and to a gate agent who can placate the flier.

"Our data problems are not a small number of big-data problems; they are a collection of thousands upon thousands of really small but interconnected problems," says Marc Fontaine, the digital transformation officer at Palantir client Airbus. Foundry's breakthrough, he says, is to "keep building and reusing the edifice that we use."

Palantir has lost its aversion to salespeople. It says it hired 35 of them this year.

The company ended last year with around $600 million of revenue and expects them to reach more than $750 million in 2018 and $1 billion in 2019, people briefed on the figures say. It has told investors it hopes to earn 70% of its revenue from corporations in the near future, from around half at present and around none in its early days.

Palantir still faces a public-perception problem. It has long drawn criticism for government work including software to help law enforcement expand surveillance of suspects. It has earned billions from U.S. government contracts across administrations of both parties, but immigration and privacy advocates have recently criticized its contracts with Immigration and Customs Enforcement, suggesting the company is helping the Trump administration's separation of migrant families.

In meetings this year, some employees begged executives to end ICE contracts. Mr. Karp told them that Palantir helps stem the cross-border flow of drugs, not separate families, and that he wouldn't make business decisions to comport with short-term politics.

"They can't run their business on the basis of the latest political thing," says Palantir investor Mr. Langone, "or they wouldn't have a business."

Mr. Karp says his family has distaste for Palantir's work and that the Trump era has made it harder to make sales pitches.

"There are lots of customers globally and some domestically who feel they do not want to be affiliated with a company that powers the clandestine agencies of the world," he says. "We want a public perception that reflects who we are, and not everyone will like it."

Some investors say they are putting increasing pressure on Palantir, which has consistently pushed back IPO plans, to go public. "It's probably time to do something public," says Tim Sullivan, chief executive of Palantir investor Oceanic Partners. On the private markets, he says, "there's not very much demand" for shares.

Palantir reports financials in an annual staff gathering and this year the meeting was held late. That alarmed employees, whom Palantir has paid partly in hard-to-sell stock tied to company performance. Engineering head Shyam Sankar later stood before staff and warned that competition was stiffening.

"The Uruk-hai are coming!" he said, referring to a villainous "Lord of the Rings" army. Palantir offered additional pay in equity to staff who stick around long-term and slashed the per-share price of such options.

Mr. Karp says new cost-cutting efforts, like a pared-back travel policy, are about avoiding waste.

Morgan Stanley investment bankers have told Palantir that if it is to go public near the high end of a $36 billion to $41 billion valuation, it must convince the market there are years of growth ahead, say people familiar with the discussions.

Morgan Stanley's mutual funds, which own Palantir shares, in filings estimate Palantir's value at under $5 billion as of June. Morgan Stanley declined to comment. Other funds peg the value higher.

Mr. Karp says Palantir's attitude toward an IPO is "completely schizophrenic." He told the Journal two years ago that an offering would allow employees "liquidity at a fair price." He now professes to have "no fixed opinion" on one.

Technology (A Special Report) --- More VC Money Put Into Fewer Deals

By Eliot Brown 
812 words
13 November 2018
The Wall Street Journal
J
R10
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

It has been a fast ride for Mexico City-based startup Grin Inc., which wants to bring shared electric scooters to market throughout Latin America.

Grin incorporated in April and launched its operations in July while at the Silicon Valley startup boot camp Y Combinator, where investors swooped in to give it a $20 million check. By October, it had raised $25 million more.

The rapid fundraising has been "amazing, as a founder," says Grin co-founder Sergio Romo, who says the investments valued the company at $182 million. "We knew if we wanted to be competitive in the space and move fast, we needed to raise a big round."

The investment frenzy that propelled Uber Technologies Inc., WeWork Cos. and other giant startups' valuations to new heights has trickled down to companies at their infancy.

But unlike the dot-com boom, when seemingly any 20-something with an idea was handed a check, investors are concentrating more money into fewer deals, leading to ballooning valuations.

The median size of seed investments -- usually the first money invested in a startup -- in the first nine months of this year in the U.S. stood at $2 million, nearly four times the $550,000 average in 2013, while the median valuation at the seed stage rose to $7 million from $4.7 million, according to data firm PitchBook. Startups in hot sectors or those run by more experienced entrepreneurs are taking on $30 million or $40 million before they've even launched their operations.

Within the past two months, one autonomous-trucking startup, Kodiak Robotics, announced a $40 million first round of investment, while another, Ike Robotics, is in talks with venture capitalists for an investment of more than $40 million, according to people familiar with the discussions. A financial-tech company called Figure Technologies, founded by Michael Cagney, the former chief executive of online lender Social Finance, raised $50 million in its first announced fundraising.

At the same time, the number of U.S. startups receiving an initial round of financing fell 40% in the first nine months of this year from the same period in 2015, to 1,594, according to PitchBook, as investors say there are fewer entrepreneurs who have viable ideas.

This price inflation for new startups is raising the stakes for investors, since companies are at their riskiest when the business isn't proven, or is only an idea.

Michael Seibel, chief executive of Y Combinator, says companies coming out of its incubator typically are valued between $6 million and $12 million. Grin was an extreme case, he says, coming at the height of a broader scooter frenzy among investors. Still, valuations and terms typically get bid up for the hottest 5% to 10% of companies in the incubator's batches, he says. That can sometimes mean raising too much money, leading to wasteful spending decisions, he says. "Sometimes founders can get carried away when they're one of the hot companies."

The emergence of small venture-capital shops is helping to propel startup valuations. While new entrants in venture capital were once rare, at least 223 U.S. firms have raised their first venture fund since 2012, according to PitchBook, bringing in more than $15 billion in new money.

These early-stage funds are popping up in increasingly esoteric areas. There are cannabis funds, artificial-intelligence funds and at least nine new real-estate-focused venture funds. Athletes like basketball star Kevin Durant and retired baseball great Derek Jeter are investing in early startups; football legend Joe Montana has a fund that invested in Grin.

For those looking for less competition, investors have -- not ironically -- coined a new term: pre-seed investing. It's essentially seed funding, in that it's the first money put into a company by outside investors, but it tends to involve smaller checks and to come even earlier in a company's life than seed funding traditionally has.

Meanwhile, some venture firms that typically invest at later stages are investing earlier to avoid colliding with SoftBank Group Corp.'s $92 billion Vision Fund and a number of other record-size growth funds being raised to back companies closer to their initial public offerings of shares.

"These megafunds have an impact that trickles down to the earlier stages," says Joe Horowitz, managing general partner at Icon Ventures, who says he finds the large checks and increased valuations concerning. Excess money is easily wasted, he says, through advertising or sales staff in an attempt to grow.

"Some markets can only grow at a certain pace no matter how much money you throw at them," he says.

---

Mr. Brown is a Wall Street Journal reporter in San Francisco. Email: eliot.brown@wsj.com.

Online lending: US fintechs gain from behaving more like banks

Ben McLannahan 
912 words
12 November 2018
05:01
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Platforms once out to change  the entire system  move away from  tech    and risk

When Noah Breslow rang the opening bell at the New York Stock Exchange in the week before Christmas 2014, it was a high point   perhaps the high point   for the fledgling online lending industry.

The company he ran, OnDeck, had grown rapidly in the years after the financial crisis by supplying quick and fuss-free credit to small businesses over the internet.

By then hundreds of similar online platforms had sprung up, trying to bypass Main Street lenders like Chase and Bank of America, which were grappling with a whole host of tougher regulations brought in after the 2008 collapse of Lehman Brothers.

One company targeting consumer loans, Lending Club of San Francisco, had made a splashy market debut two weeks before OnDeck, talking of transforming  the entire banking system, making it more transparent, more cost efficient, more consumer friendly .

Much of the excitement has evaporated. Rising delinquencies on some classes of loans and a governance scandal at Lending Club have given the entire industry a rough ride.

Talk of putting big brick-and-mortar lenders like Citibank and Bank of America out of business has turned to working with them, providing ready-made parcels of loans the banks can hold, while supplying software so that banks can originate loans to their existing customers more efficiently.

Meanwhile, most of the platforms have slashed sales teams, while increasing staff in their compliance and legal departments, as regulators have begun to pay more attention to the potential threats the platforms pose to financial stability.

Mr Breslow, a fast-talking Bostonian, says the entire online lending industry is better, and more durable, for that humbling experience. OnDeck itself has made money for its shareholders in three of the last four quarters, and has seen its shares more than double since a trough in May last year.

 2015 to 2017 was a pretty interesting time,  says the 43-year-old chief executive, from OnDeck s headquarters on Broadway in New York.  But we made some aggressive changes. 

Tech-focused investors were primed for rapid revenue growth, and when it failed to materialise, they sold the stock.

Key to the turnround was a realisation that the original  marketplace  structure had to change. Under that model, also known as  peer-to-peer,  companies like OnDeck tried to act like simple matchmakers, connecting applicants with investors who liked the look of their credit profile, in exchange for an upfront fee.

Back in the summer of 2015, some 40 per cent OnDeck s loans were funded that way. But that left it vulnerable to withdrawals of investors when market sentiment soured, leaving the company with a mountain of fixed costs to cover with patchy income.

Now, the company has shifted almost entirely to signing long-term supply deals with institutions such as banks and life insurance companies. Every loan that comes in through the door is effectively already committed to be sold.

OnDeck has aimed to overhaul its shareholder register, meanwhile, going out of its way to attract finance-focused investors, rather than tech accounts that wanted to see gung-ho growth every quarter.

OnDeck s skew towards the latter kind of investor was a byproduct of its initial public offering, when  fintech  was among the hottest themes on Wall Street. Back then, underwriters had courted tech-focused fund managers with prospectuses laden with Silicon Valley buzzwords like  total addressable market  and  proprietary algorithms that leverage behavioural data .

Investors like these were primed for rapid revenue growth, and when this failed to materialise, they sold the stock. OnDeck s peak-to-trough decline in its share price was 86 per cent; Lending Club s was 90 per cent. However much the companies protested that slower top-line growth was actually a positive   implying that they were being more selective on lending   investors were unpersuaded.

Over the past couple of years OnDeck has spent more time at investor conferences focused on financial stocks, while cultivating more of a following among financials-focused analysts.

The rewards are coming through, says one institutional investor who spoke on condition of anonymity. The problem was not the business   which is sound   but  the messaging , he says, which was falling on deaf ears.

I still think we are a fintech but a  tech  is a stretchNoah Breslow, OnDeck

Mr Breslow is happier with the investor mix now and how results are received. In the second quarter, for example, OnDeck reported a slight fall in loan originations, from $591m to $587m. But investors welcomed a relatively low net charge-off rate   a measure of bad debt   of 11.2 per cent, from 18.6 per cent a year earlier.

In the third quarter originations were back up and the charge-off rate edged down further, to 11.1 per cent. Shares in the company bounced almost one-third following the results   the biggest one-day move since the IPO.

 I still think we are a fintech but a  tech  is a stretch,  says Mr Breslow.  We acknowledge we have 	. 	. 	. 	credit risk and liquidity risk, which are hallmarks of financial companies,  he adds.  But at the same time I think we have a very differentiated 	. 	. 	. 	model at the core, which is powered by original technology and original analytics. Over time we expect companies in this business to be primarily valued by profits.

Elliott and Veritas team up to buy Athenahealth for $5.7bn

Eric Platt, Mark Vandevelde and James Fontanella-Khan in New York 
463 words
12 November 2018
16:50
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Deal values US healthcare technology group at 12% premium to last closing share price

Private equity firm Veritas Capital and hedge fund Elliott Management have agreed to buy US healthcare technology group Athenahealth for $5.7bn. The cash deal, announced Monday morning, values the provider of electronic healthcare record and medical billing software at $135 a share.

Elliott, which owns about 9 per cent of Athenahealth, will hold a minority stake via its Evergreen Coast Capital private equity arm.

For Veritas, which has some $10bn of assets under management, the buyout will be among the largest ever completed by the New York-based group.

The private equity fund has made a string of acquisitions over the past several years, including the purchase of a GE Healthcare unit in July for just under $1.1bn, which it intends to combine with its new acquisition.

Jeff Immelt, the former chairman and chief executive of General Electric who joined Athenahealth as chairman in February, said the  agreement with Veritas 	. 	. 	. 	maximises value for our shareholders and accelerates our goal to transform healthcare.  The $5.7bn price tag is based on Athenahealth s fully diluted share count.

The deal comes five months after Jonathan Bush, a co-founder of the company, resigned as chief executive after damaging revelations surrounding his personal life.

Mr Bush had been embroiled in a bitter tussle with Elliot, which was trying to boot him out of his role, arguing he was mismanaging the company.

Elliott approached Athenahealth with a $160-per-share takeover offer in May, a year after taking a stake in the company, but talks fizzled as the company s shares proved volatile. The stock rose as high as $164 in the wake of the Elliott offer in May but it closed at $120.35 on Friday, taking its decline for the year to nearly 10 per cent.

The activist fund lambasted the board for  fail[ing] to correct a host of identifiable operational issues . It said Athenahealth, which was one of the biggest winners from rules introduced under the Obama administration that required doctors to move their paper files over to computerised systems, was doomed to underperformance as a public company.

Some analysts said clunky technology left the company vulnerable to competition from better technologies using big data and artificial intelligence to help guide physicians. Last week, Athenahealth said third-quarter revenue fell short of analyst forecasts.

Elliott is run by Paul Singer, the activist investor whose decade-long battle over defaulted bonds issued by the government of Argentina at one point involved detaining one of the country s navy ships while it was docked in a Ghanaian port.

Movers and shakers, November 12

Chris Flood

12 November 2018
03:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English

Legal & General Investment Management has appointed Sonja Laud as deputy chief investment officer. Ms Laud previously served as head of equity at Fidelity International. William Riley has also joined as head of solutions for LGIM, the 985bn asset management arm of Legal & General. Mr Riley has moved from BlackRock.AQR, the $226bn Connecticut hedge fund manager, has a new head of research engineering. Howard Mansell previously served as head of engineering for artificial intelligence research at Facebook, the social network. AQR said last month that it had started an engineering centre in Bangalore, India, and expects to hire 150 quantitative engineers and data scientists. Chrys Kattirisetti has joined as country head for India from DE Shaw, the US hedge fund manager.

Investec Asset Management has hired Marco Tang as head of adviser business for the greater China region, a new role based in Hong Kong. Mr Tang joins from Neuberger Berman. 

DWS, the  692bn asset manager owned by Deutsche Bank, has recruited Thomas Kalthfer as an investment director in its global infrastructure business. Mr Kalthfer, who will be based in London, previously worked for Barclays.

Ed Collinge has moved to Robeco as global head of insurance strategy, a new role at the  167bn Dutch asset manager. Mr Collinge previously worked in a similar role at Invesco, the US asset manager.

Standard Life Aberdeen, the $735bn Edinburgh-based asset manager, has hired Aymeric Forest as global head of multi-asset investing. Mr Forest will join in February from Schroders where he worked in a similar role. He will replace the veteran Guy Stern who has decided to retire in 2019.

Benjamin Day has moved to American Century to lead UK institutional business for the $162bn Kansas City asset manager. Mr Day previously worked for Stratton Street Capital.

There are two arrivals in the infrastructure team at UBS Asset Management. Patrick Cunningham joins as head of infrastructure business development for the Americas from McCarvill Capital Partners. Viktor Kozel has moved to the role of portfolio manager in the infrastructure debt team in London from Deutsche Bank.

Resco, a new London-based boutique asset manager, will launch its inaugural fund this month.The Resco macro credit fund has secured commitments of $50m and hopes to raise up to $1bn. Resco was co-founded by David Ric and Alex Eventon who both previously served as senior portfolio managers at Amundi and Lombard Odier respectively, along with Scott Schuberg, the former chief executive of Rivkin, the Australian financial services provider.

Charges for this advertising service are based on the number of lines published and the classification of the fund. Please contact data@ft.com or call +44 (0)20 7873 3132. The funds published on these pages are grouped together by fund management company.

Prices are in pence unless otherwise indicated and those designated $ with no prefix refer to US dollars. Yields % allow for buying expenses. Prices of certain older insurance linked plans are subject to capital gains tax on sales. Some Property Unit Trusts are limited to investors who are UK tax exempt.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment ManagementAssociation, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in theUK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors .

Single price: Based onamid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available .

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge fromfund managers/operators.

Business
China's Race to Dominate AI; In an interview, Kai-Fu Lee also discusses why he thinks U.S. tech giants will never succeed in expanding in China

By Yun-Hee Kim 
1,388 words
12 November 2018
15:47
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The U.S. may be winning the race in artificial intelligence for now. But it won't last for long.

So predicts Kai-Fu Lee, chairman and chief executive of Sinovation Ventures, a Beijing-based venture-capital firm. He believes the U.S.'s current technological edge over China could disappear within five years, thanks to government support, a growing force of entrepreneurs and their speed of execution.

Mr. Lee, who previously was the head of Google Inc. in China, recently spoke with The Wall Street Journal about the AI race, entrepreneurship in China, and prospects for leading U.S. tech companies in the world's most populous country. Edited excerpts follow.

China vs. U.S.

WSJ: How do Chinese and U.S. strategies to develop AI differ?

MR. LEE: China's techno-utilitarian policy encourages technology to be launched first, observed, and allowed to proceed without obstruction if things go smoothly. If there are issues, it could quickly come up with regulations. An example is mobile payments. In the U.S., this might raise all kinds of lobby, security, hacking concerns from the credit-card companies and banks. But in China, Tencent and Alibaba were permitted to try to run payment systems as software companies, and they did a great job. Mobile payment took over in China.

In China, a highway is being built with sensors to improve autonomous driving, new cities are being developed with autonomous vehicles; some with two layers of roads one layer for pedestrians, pets, bicycles, and another layer for cars. All of these will help with testing for autonomous-vehicle systems to be launched sooner. With AI applications, you need a lot of data to do testing. China has a lot of data, so its approach allows it to move faster.

WSJ: What do you think the U.S. needs to improve on and where does China need to invest further when it comes to AI?

MR. LEE: It's going to be about who has more data, faster entrepreneurs, lots of AI engineers and government support. China will continue to catch up against the U.S. in terms of building products and monetizing. What could work in the U.S.'s favor is if someone disrupts the whole thing with a brand new fundamental technological change.

WSJ: Which country is ahead?

MR. LEE: In internet AI, which is algorithms making profitable recommendations for people based on their Web browsing history, China and the U.S. are about equal. China will probably get ahead because it has more user data. In business AI, where companies mine their customer data to come up with new product ideas and improve service, or use it to monitor systems to make them more efficient or lucrative, the U.S. is ahead, and will probably stay ahead because its enterprise data is properly archived and more usable for AI. In perception AI, or things like facial recognition and other biometric interfaces, China is ahead because it is building more sensors cheaply and for broader uses, and it will probably get further ahead.

In autonomous vehicles, it's incredibly hard to tell because it depends on policy. The question is, is the road ahead to making autonomous vehicles ubiquitous mostly about technology breakthroughs? Then the U.S. is ahead. If it's a matter of rapidly testing and evolving without policy, lobbying and unions holding it back, it would be China in the lead.

Work and the workplace

WSJ: What jobs will disappear because of AI?

MR. LEE: Customer service, but not every kind. Customer service with very high-end human touch will stay. Telemarketing and telesales will disappear. Dish washing, fruit picking, assembly-line inspection will all disappear. Paralegals and accountants but not 100%. Some lawyers who do form filling, those would be replaced.

Creativity-oriented jobs are safe. Working in a construction environment is safe. Cleaning is hard to do for a robot and every house is different, so that's safe.

WSJ: China is known for its intense startup work culture. It's often referred to as 996 work 9 a.m. to 9 p.m., six days a week. Is this a critical factor to the success of Chinese startups? Can this work culture foster true innovation?

MR. LEE: The most important things are focus, tenacity, operational excellence, speed of execution and hard work. They all come together. If you are just hard working but working dumb, that's not good. Then you get burned out. I'm personally concerned about my entrepreneurs but I don't have much hope of changing them. This culture cannot be changed in the next two to three decades as China still emerges from its current state.

WSJ: Do you think Chinese tech companies will be more successful because entrepreneurs there do the grunt work?

MR. LEE: The Chinese companies are not nearly as breakthrough-innovative as the many Silicon Valley greats. Whether it's Elon Musk or Steve Jobs. The whole Chinese education system doesn't really easily train these types of brilliant thinkers. But on the other hand, I think if Silicon Valley or the U.S. continues to view China as copycats, then it will miss many opportunities. The stereotype of looking at Chinese companies as lower-quality copycats will cause American entrepreneurs to have blinders on and miss stuff they should learn.

Chinese innovation

WSJ: Is there real innovation coming out of China now?

MR. LEE: Absolutely. WeChat [a payment and messaging platform from Tencent] is an example. If you look at ByteDance [the company that operates the news-aggregation app Toutiao], while traditional media may not like the content that's being put through, it's a very innovative way for displaying content. Think about minivideos Vine [a video app that was owned by Twitter] never worked out, but Chinese companies are doing great with minivideos. Bike-sharing apps like Mobike, these are innovative, creative business solutions that add value, but they weren't created the Silicon Valley way.

WSJ: Trade tensions between China and the U.S. are making it more difficult for foreign companies in China and Chinese companies looking to expand in the U.S. What are the prospects for U.S. tech companies in China?

MR. LEE: It's almost irrelevant. The U.S. and China are almost parallel universes. American companies will not succeed in China, with or without significant regulation. Same for Chinese companies in the U.S. We're almost at a point, at least in consumer internet and AI companies, where U.S. companies don't fit the ecosystem. Let's say ByteDance wants to come to the U. S. how are they going to get people to trust this brand? How will it answer questions about the newsfeed?

WSJ: So you don't think the Chinese government is making it more difficult for foreign companies to operate there? Or is it more an issue that U.S. companies built products that don't work for consumers in China?

MR. LEE: I actually think Google and Facebook will get some element of their products in China. But I don't think they will be successful, just because of the parallel-universe reason. Clearly, they are talking [about expanding into China] and Waymo [the autonomous-driving unit of Alphabet Inc.] got approval in Shanghai. Where American companies are likely to have a chance would be to work in a simple, nontangled part of this parallel universe such as doing something brand new.

Ms. Kim is a technology editor for The Wall Street Journal in New York. She can be reached at "mailto:yun-hee.kim@wsj.com".

Good day. SAP has agreed to acquire Qualtrics Inc. for $8 billion. The move takes the enterprise software startup off the market days before it was set to debut as a public company, echoing a similar scenario in January 2017, when Cisco Systems Inc. snatched up AppDynamics Inc. just before its own initial public offering.

VC winners in the SAP deal include Accel and Sequoia Capital, which first invested in Qualtrics in 2012, pumping in $35 million each at a $350 million valuation, according to a person familiar with the figures.

Accel and Sequoia own 16% and 9% of Qualtrics, respectively. Insight Venture Partners owns 15% after investing in a subsequent round of financing.

Utah-based Qualtrics had a $2.5 billion valuation in April 2017, after raising $180 million.

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

Crypto reboot: As an early cryptocurrency-focused firm, Pantera Capital racked up wins from investments in bitcoin and parlayed them into a crypto portfolio with hot startups. But Pantera's algorithmic Digital Asset Fund, one of the firm's newest funds, recently shifted its approach to rely on a more hands-on selection from its team after poor performance this year amid a collapse in the crypto market. Despite Pantera's early gains in its venture and bitcoin funds, the change presents a challenge for the firm, coming as Pantera works on raising multiple new crypto and venture funds. WSJ Pro's Tomio Geron has the exclusive.

Test for biotech unicorns: Moderna Inc., a $7 billion developer of new messenger RNA therapeutics, revealed plans to go public in what promises to be the most closely watched biotech IPO in recent memory. Biotech startups have been herded into the public markets recently as growth-hungry investors bet heavily on small drugmakers. WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley has more.

Next act: Nearly three months after leaving as GE Ventures' head of health-care technology investments, Lisa Suennen has chosen a new career path. Ms. Suennen will lead the venture fund of Manatt Phelps & Phillips, a legal and consulting firm. Additionally, she will head the firm's digital technology business, which helps companies strategically implement new technologies and protocols. WSJ Pro's Heather Mack has more.

Roof's Take: Unicorns Stampede in Breath-Taking Numbers

By Katie Roof

Startup valuations are hitting the billion-dollar mark in record numbers with still with no end in sight.

So far this year, 44 companies have joined the Billion Dollar Startup Club, according to PitchBook Data. This surpasses the 43 so-called unicorns that were newly minted in all of 2015, the previous record.

The year's entrants include scooter company Bird Rides Inc. and food delivery specialist DoorDash Inc., which both raised multiple rounds of capital in 2018.

While some view the trend as a sign that the industry has plenty of businesses ripe with promise, others worry that investors may be getting carried away.

Former Fed Vice Chair Voices Concern at Loosening Regulations | Fed Plans to Ease Stress Tests | Trump Administration to Drive Shift at Fannie, Freddie; The Wall Street Journal's central banking newsletter for Monday, November 12, 2018
