WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

The U.S. private sector added 227,000 jobs in October, according to a report released Wednesday, largely driven by employment growth at larger businesses.

The ADP National Employment report measures the change in nonfarm private sector employment in the U.S. Economists polled by The Wall Street Journal were expecting 180,000 jobs to be added.

The 227,000 new jobs are the most the private sector has added since February of this year when it added 241,000 jobs, according to data from the ADP Research Institute and Moody's Analytics, which put together the monthly report.

Large businesses, or those that have at least 500 employees, added 102,000 in October, according to data from the ADP Research Institute and Moody's Analytics, which put together the monthly report. Medium-size businesses, or those with between 50 and 499 employees, added 96,000 jobs.

"We continue to see larger employers benefit in this environment as they are more apt to provide the competitive wages and strong benefits employees desire," said Ahu Yildirmaz, co-head of the ADP Research Institute, in prepared remarks. He added that "the only blemish is the struggles small businesses are having filling open job positions."

The service sector was again the biggest beneficiary of the new jobs, adding 189,000.

Within the service industry, trade, transportation and utilities added 61,000 jobs, the most of any of the sector subcategories.

Business
Capital One's Tech Transformation; Capital One CIO Rob Alexander on the challenges and advantages of a bank operating as a technology company

By Sara Castellanos 
1,482 words
30 October 2018
02:08
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Capital One Financial Corp. wants to be both a technology company and a bank.

Since 2011, Chief Information Officer Rob Alexander has expanded his technology staff to 9,000 from 2,500, hiring hundreds of software engineers, developers and artificial-intelligence experts to develop products such as a recently launched digital chatbot.

He also helped spearhead an effort over the past three years to move the bank's back-end software development tools and infrastructure to the public cloud. That way, he says, the bank's technical staff can get software products to market faster, more easily and with top-notch security.

The rate at which Capital One is relying on the cloud, embracing technology and competing for AI talent is "surprising and remarkable," says Lex Sokolin, global director of fintech strategy for financial-company research firm Autonomous Research.

Mr. Alexander spoke with The Wall Street Journal about the development of the bank's chatbot, Eno, and the challenges and advantages of operating like a technology company. Edited excerpts follow.

Understanding intent

WSJ: Did you run into any challenges creating the Eno chatbot?

MR. ALEXANDER: In 2016 we experimented with and piloted a version of Eno that was built on a commercial third-party natural language processing AI engine. We quickly came to the conclusion that while the performance was good, it really has to be great for customers to use it.

The off-the-shelf natural language processing models weren't good enough, and we had to start from scratch and build our own because we care about accuracy in two ways. One, Eno needed the ability to understand the intent of a request, and then the functionality to execute on that intent. The first version of Eno didn't understand the intent well enough.

For example, we calculated 2,500 ways a customer might request his or her balance, including misspellings and things that autocorrect does. The model is constantly learning what those variations are and what the customer's intent was.

WSJ: What have you changed about Eno over time based on customer feedback?

MR. ALEXANDER: If the chatbot can't help customers with things that are important to them, or they get generic responses, they won't use it.

A lot of customers ask Eno to remind them before their bill is due on their card. We have trained Eno to understand that request, and then automate the process of setting up reminders for customers in advance of when their bill is due.

Other examples of that proactivity would be if we see fraudulent or suspicious activity on your account, Eno can automatically reach out to you through the mobile app, SMS text or email. If we see a double charge for the same merchant right away or an unusually big tip on a restaurant bill out of pattern, Eno will send a notification or email or SMS text to a customer that says, "Hey, this looks suspicious, did you mean to do that?"

WSJ: You said there are 2,500 ways a customer might request their balance. What common customer phrasings did it not recognize before that it does now?

MR. ALEXANDER: Some examples of this include "How much money is in my account?" This is an example of an inquiry that doesn't directly use the word "balance," but Eno understands the intent behind the question.

"How much have I racked up on my card?" This is an example where a customer is speaking in natural language, in a way they would think about balance, despite the context being a bit unclear.

WSJ: Can you provide some examples of things that Eno wasn't doing well that you changed, based on feedback?

MR. ALEXANDER: As we started in our web application, we noticed customers typing in very lengthy messages. This would confuse Eno, due to multiple intents, and extraneous information. So we did a design change: Up front, Eno says that it does better with shorter questions. This reduced customer friction, and Eno has responded better. Typically, we prefer to adapt the tech to the human behavior, but in this case we saw an opportunity to influence human behavior in a way that set both Eno and the customer up for success while the technology continues to evolve.

WSJ: What makes Eno different as a result of it being created by an in-house team of technologists?

MR. ALEXANDER: One important point to call out is that Eno wasn't just built by a team of in-house technologists. It was built by an integrated team of product, tech and design professionals, all contributing to the project's success from start to finish.

Eno's personality is a great example of the outcomes from that operating model. When we set out to design the intelligent assistant, we recognized that character development was a capability we didn't have so we hired filmmakers, anthropologists, journalists and designers with deep experience in character development to help define and shape how our intelligent assistant would connect with our customers. Today, we have an incredibly talented in-house AI design team that is deeply focused on creating experiences that lead to contextually relevant, meaningful conversations and relationships rooted in trust, empathy and understanding.

For example, our decision to design Eno as a gender-neutral intelligent assistant was made by that internal team.

Talent competition

WSJ: What's an example of a challenge you've had to overcome in the transition toward operating more like a technology company?

MR. ALEXANDER: It's really about how to attract and retain the type of talent that you need, and in quantities that are required to fulfill on that mission of operating like a technology company. Today we hire software engineers, data engineers, machine learning and cloud infrastructure and cyber engineering. That's the toughest challenge in this whole thing, because you're trying to attract a kind of talent that's in high demand in the marketplace.

WSJ: What are you doing to attract that talent?

MR. ALEXANDER: We have to offer competitive compensation and benefits, and we benchmark ourselves against tech companies and their compensation. Our founder is still the CEO, and there's that ethos that you get with a founder-led organization. It feels much more like a startup. We're also very focused on providing an environment that allows people to learn and build their skills.

WSJ: You oversee 9,000 technology staff. As a bank, why do you need that many technologists?

MR. ALEXANDER: We recognized that customers ultimately are going to choose financial institutions that offer the same kind of experiences they can get in other domains in their digital lives. We need to operate like technology companies that are delivering those experiences in other dimensions of their lives, which means we need to be building our own software solutions, and doing that requires a highly technical workforce. Just like Amazon, Netflix or Uber need to have great engineering technology staffs to build their products, we need to have the same thing for banking.

WSJ: What are some areas of opportunity that you've identified for using machine learning to serve customers better?

MR. ALEXANDER: Fraud is a really interesting problem. The hard part from a technology perspective is you have to make decisions in real time. We've got a customer standing there at point-of-sale and there is this 50-millisecond requirement to return an answer on "do we approve this transaction?"

You've got to have really sophisticated technology to deal with the volume of data and deal with it in real time, and you want to apply complex algorithms to distinguish those transactions that are abnormal and out of pattern and look like fraud. But you've got to tune it well because you don't want to decline a real transaction. We've been investing heavily in this.

Another machine-learning example is a feature we've developed as part of our auto-finance business. You take an image of a car that is either a photo that you take or you can hold up your phone with your camera in real time and point it at a car that you're interested in and we can automatically in real time tell you what your monthly payment would be on that vehicle.

Ms. Castellanos is a Wall Street Journal reporter in New York. Email her at "mailto:sara.castellanos.wsj.com".

VC Daily: Longevity Backer ARCH Is Refueling; Bold Fee Structures for New Funds

1,190 words
30 October 2018
12:14
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Good day. So-called longevity startups, which are developing treatments that target specific conditions linked to the aging process, have been having a moment lately. We have a story today about a new fundraising by ARCH Venture Partners, one of the chief investors behind the scenes of the anti-aging movement.

We'll also share exclusive details about the customs-evasion tactics used by some gig workers helping to deliver items to shoppers abroad. Plus, Yuliya Chernova's Take today raises some urgent questions about the large funds and fee structures that first-time venture firms are running up the flagpole.

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

Bio boost: Bio-focused ARCH Venture Partners, an early investor in longevity startup Unity Biotechnology Inc. and gene-sequencing specialist Illumina Inc., is in the market to raise up to $600 million for what would be the firm's 10th and potentially largest fund. WSJ Pro's Heather Mack has more.

Delivery duty: Online companies like San Francisco-based Grabr that hook up travelers willing to deliver items to shoppers abroad are building a new industry, but their users often participate in an age-old tradition: customs evasion. The Wall Street Journal interviewed more than a dozen Grabr travelers, almost all of whom said they tried to bring expensive items through customs without paying duties. WSJ's Andrea Fuller has the exclusive.

Scooter deal-maker: Less than three months after announcing an investment from Uber Technologies Inc., electric-scooter startup Lime has landed one of its key business executives. Lime hired David Richter as chief of business, where he will oversee mergers and acquisitions, sales strategy and other finance operations. He held a similar role at Uber for more than four years. WSJ's Greg Bensinger has the exclusive.

Chernova's Take: Taking on Bigger Bets, and Charging More for It

By Yuliya Chernova

Here's one way to summarize the hot venture market of 2018: VCs have been raising larger funds, and some of them doing so faster than usual, based on paper gains. Higher VC fund fees and investments in unproven expensive sectors like crypto are becoming more common.

Joshua Kushner's Thrive Capital just closed $1 billion for a pair of funds on mostly unrealized performance. The firm charged 2.5% in management fees, compared to the 2% typical fee, as WSJ Pro Venture Capital previously reported.

At least Thrive Capital already has a few funds under its belt, but some new firms expect a similar fee structure.

In this market, however, that money easily flows from LPs to VCs and then on to the entrepreneurs. Large bets are now the norm, which exposes VCs to potentially large losses if any one company goes under.

More in Chernova's Take.

Exits

Technology-enabled health-care network Carbon Health has merged with Direct Urgent Care, a network of urgent care centers. San Francisco-based Carbon is backed by investors including Builders VC, Javelin Venture Partners and Two Sigma Ventures.

VC Funds

Define Ventures is raising a first fund of up to $65 million, according to a regulatory filing. The San Francisco-based firm was founded this year and has secured $50 million in committed capital as of Monday. Lynne Chou O'Keefe, a partner at Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers who handles health-care investments, is listed as the managing director of Define Ventures. Ms. Chou O'Keefe declined to comment on the filing.

Circulate Capital is raising a $90 million fund to invest in companies and infrastructure across Southern Asia dedicated to combating the ocean plastic crisis. Limited partners expected to invest in the fund include PepsiCo, Procter & Gamble, Dow, Danone, Unilever and Coca-Cola Co.

South African technology group Naspers Ltd. has committed 1.4 billion South African rand, ($95 million) to Naspers Foundry, which will back tech startups in that country.

Work-Bench Ventures, a New York-based venture firm focused on enterprise technologies, has raised $46.2 million for its second fund, according to a regulatory filing.

Moonrise Capital is seeking to raise up to $200 million for Moonrise Venture Partners II LP, according to a regulatory filing. Donald Pascal, a managing partner at Moonrise, appears on the document.

New Money

Enable Injections Inc., a Cincinnati-based developer of wearable devices for the subcutaneous delivery of biologics and other high-volume therapeutics, has completed the first close of a round targeted at $50 million. Sanofi is leading the funding, with participation from investors including ORI Healthcare Fund, CincyTech, Cincinnati Children's Hospital, Cintrifuse, Ohio Innovation Fund and Cleveland Clinic. "http://www.enableinjections.com"

StarkWare, an Israeli blockchain startup, has secured a $30 million Series A round. Paradigm led the investment, which included participation from Intel Capital, Sequoia Capital, Atomico, DCVC, Consensys Ventures, Coinbase Ventures and Floodgate. Matt Huang of Paradigm will join the company's board. "https://www.starkware.co"

Concord Worldwide Inc., a San Francisco-based contract management company, has closed $25 million in Series B funding. Tenaya Capital led the round, with participation from previous investors CRV and Alven Capital. Tenaya Partner Paul Drews will join the company's board. "https://www.concordnow.com"

LoopMe Inc., a mobile marketing startup, has fetched a $17 million investment led by BGF. HV Holtzbrinck Ventures, Harbert European Growth Capital, Open Ocean and Impulse VC also participated. Sarah Ledwidge of BGF joins the company's board. The company has offices in London and New York, among other cities. "https://loopme.com"

Carrot Inc., a Redwood City, Calif.-based smoking cessation program provider, has dug up $25 million in funding. Johnson & Johnson Innovation   JJDC Inc. led the round, and was joined by investors including New York Life Ventures, Khosla Ventures and R7 Partners. "https://pivot.co"

Waterline Data Inc., a Mountain View, Calif.-based data cataloging provider, has raised $14.5 million in Series C funding. Menlo Ventures led the round, with participation from Jackson Square Ventures, Partech Ventures and Infosys. Robert Youngjohns, a senior adviser at McKinsey & Co. and operating partner at HgCapital, will join the company's board. "https://www.waterlinedata.com"

AnyMind Group, a Singapore-based provider of artificial intelligence technology, has secured $13.4 million in funding. LINE Corp., Mirai Creation Fund, Jafco Asia and Dream Incubator were among the investors. "https://anymindgroup.com"

WiredScore, a New York-based company that evaluates and rates office buildings for the speed and reliability of their internet connections, has raised $9 million in Series A funding. Investors including Bessemer Venture Partners, Fifth Wall Ventures, Sterling VC, KingSett Capital and Savitt Partners provided the round. "https://wiredscore.com"

C-Suite Strategies (A Special Report) --- A Bank That Also Wants to Be a Tech Company: The CIO of Capital One Financial talks about the creation of Eno, its chatbot

By Sara Castellanos 
1,339 words
30 October 2018
The Wall Street Journal

English

Southeast Asian tech hubs race to become the next Silicon Valley

Nikkei Asian Review reporters 
4,723 words
29 October 2018
13:16
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Investment and government initiatives ignite start-up scenes from Singapore to Penang

South-east Asia s tech start-up scene is getting hotter by the year. Money is pouring in and valuations are soaring as investors bet big on the region s rapid growth, huge markets and youthful, mobile-savvy consumers.

With so much at stake, cities from Singapore to Vietnam s Ho Chi Minh City are competing to become the next Silicon Valley   home not only to successful start-ups but also to the mentors, investors and institutions that support them.

The Nikkei Asian Review looked at criteria ranging from number of start-ups to cost of office space and selected the five cities below as the hottest places for innovation in south-east Asia.

Singapore

Number of tech start-ups

4,300 as of 2016

Government support

Programmes providing equity funding, preferential loans or R&D financing, access to co-working space and incubation facilities, tax breaks for start-ups and investors, preferential immigration policies for foreign talent

Education/talent pool

National University of Singapore, ranked 11th in the 2019 QS World University Rankings, Nanyang Technological University, Singapore Institute of Technology, Nanyang Business School and Insead s Singapore campus

Access to funding

Wide range of investors, including individual angels, family office funds, hedge funds, private equity, venture capital; government programmes typically match private sector-led funding

Transportation

Transportation infrastructure among world s best; Changi Airport less than 30 minutes by taxi from most business, residential districts

Office rent

$7.20 per sq ft per month for Grade A office in central business district in July-September quarter, according to JLL

In early 2015, Sinuhe Arroyo spotted a chance for his artificial intelligence start-up to win a contract with a government agency in Singapore. But his colleagues warned him against it, since their company, Taiger, though based in Singapore, was incorporated in Spain.

Mr Arroyo, the company s founder and chief executive, pressed ahead anyway   and was pleasantly surprised when Taiger became the first foreign company to win a contract from the Singaporean government.

 My colleagues had opposed 	. 	. 	. 	bidding, saying it would be a waste of time. But I insisted Singapore is such a transparent meritocracy that we have a chance to win a contract if we have a convincing technology. I was right,  recalls Mr Arroyo.

The Taiger team had moved to Singapore from the US at the end of 2014 because they saw a less-cultivated, more transparent and faster-growing business technology market in Asia.

This article is from the Nikkei Asian Review, a global publication with a uniquely Asian perspectives on politics, the economy, business and international affairs. Our own correspondents and outside commentators from around the world share their views on Asia, while our Asia300 section provides in-depth coverage of 300 of the biggest and fastest-growing listed companies from 11 economies outside Japan.

Subscribe | Group subscriptions

In 2016, after receiving two more government tenders, Taiger reincorporated in Singapore. The following year, Singapore s start-up-backing agency SGInnovate invested in Taiger in its first fundraising round, alongside local private equity firm Tembusu Partners.

Today Taiger is a darling of Singapore s AI start-up scene. Its clients include such global companies as AIA Group, Banco Santander, Bank of America Merrill Lynch and Standard Chartered. And Mr Arroyo advises government panels on national AI strategies.

Long established as a financial centre, Singapore has become the leading start-up hub in south-east Asia thanks to a mix of ready capital and government policies. According to Enterprise Singapore, the number of start-ups in the city-state increased from about 2,800 in 2003 to 4,300 in 2016. Venture capital funding jumped from $80m in 2010 to $1.2bn in 2017, according to KPMG.

Singaporean venture capitalist Yinglan Tan sees a  lifetime opportunity  in his home country. He left Sequoia Capital last year to found his own venture capital firm, Insignia Ventures Partners, based in Singapore.

He sees plenty of room for growth, noting that south-east Asia s total tech industry market cap is roughly $20bn, compared with $300bn in India, where the GDP per capita of $1,700 is less than half of south-east Asia s $3,800.  South-east Asia s tech industry should be at least $100bn, which presents a huge opportunity for various types of start-ups and VCs,  Mr Tan said.

Singapore is attracting global investors who want to use it as a base to invest in south-east Asia s tech sector, mainly because of the country s rule of law and tax regime, Mr Tan said. It is also attracting entrepreneurs and other talent thanks to its ease of doing businesses, quality of life and high level of education, he added.

 Lots of US-educated fellow Singaporeans are coming back,  Mr Tan said. Some non-native entrepreneurs like Mr Arroyo, a Spanish citizen, are choosing Singapore as the place for incorporation as well.

As a Sequoia partner, Mr Tan lived in Silicon Valley, which has thrived with an ecosystem of angel investors, entrepreneurs turned mentors, venture capitalists, lawyers and banks. He says that Singapore is rapidly developing those supporting functions as well.  Five years ago there was almost none of those. Now we see a critical mass of mentors and supporters,  Mr Tan said.

One of those crucial elements is incubation space. The Singaporean government has been proactive in fostering this aspect as well.

In 2011 the National University of Singapore launched a start-up-incubation space called Block 71 in a renovated industrial building. Taking its name from its street address, Block 71 adopted a similar strategy to 500 Startups, the pioneering incubator launched a year earlier in Silicon Valley.

Today Block 71 is a cluster of seven incubation buildings set on a 16,000 sq m piece of land. It hosts 250 start-ups and 30 incubators and VCs   making it one of the largest clusters of start-ups in the world.

Michael Yap, a Block 71-based venture capitalist who worked in Prime Minister Lee Hsien Loong s office when Block 71 was launched, says Singaporeans still need to  think harder  about how the city-state should adapt to change.

For example, if Indonesia   the region s largest economy and home to four  unicorns    accelerates reforms and becomes more business-friendly, it is unclear whether Singapore could maintain its advantage as a regional hub for entrepreneurs and investors.

 We should think about what roles we can play in different environments so that people would not forget about Singapore and jump to Indonesia,  Yap said.

By Ken Koyanagi, Nikkei Asian Review editor-at-large

Jakarta

Number of tech start-ups

Government aiming for 1,000 start-ups by 2020

Government support

Grants for incubators and simplified tax procedures for ecommerce companies with annual turnover below a certain amount

Education/talent pool

University of Indonesia and Bandung Institute of Technology are among Indonesia s most prestigious schools

Access to funding

Large international investors like SoftBank, Temasek and Google, as well as various local venture capital investors

Transportation

Soekarno-Hatta International Airport connects various Asian cities; mass rapid transit system under construction to alleviate chronic traffic jams, with partial operation to start in 2019

Office rent

368,349 rupiah ($24.25) per sq m per month for premium space in central business district, 302,703 rupiah for Grade A space, according to JLL Indonesia

Indonesia s capital may not be the easiest place to do business   the city is plagued by chronic traffic jams and there is plenty of red tape. But despite the hassles, Jakarta has recently become one of south-east Asia s most exciting start-up hubs.

Indonesia boasts four unicorns   start-ups valued at $1bn or more   and it is the second-largest recipient of venture capital in south-east Asia, behind only Singapore. Venture capital firms pumped $1.4bn into 123 deals in 2016, double the value of their commitments a year earlier, according to consultancy AT Kearney and Google. Deal values more than likely doubled again in 2017.

 Now we are seeing a much more consistent rise of start-ups,  said Ridzki Syahputera, corporate development manager at Convergence Ventures, a local venture capital firm.  I think a lot of it is market confidence that they will get funding. Entrepreneurs believe that they can get funding, whereas five years ago, they might have thought, only if I am really the best would I be able to successfully fundraise. 

It is Indonesia s market potential that has international investors excited. The country has the world s fourth-largest population, of more than 260m people, 60 per cent of whom are under 40. They are also digital-savvy: smartphone penetration is set to reach 78 per cent in 2020.

Unlike countries with smaller domestic markets like Singapore or South Korea, Indonesia s start-up scene is focused on the consumer sector. Its unicorns are all consumer-centric: Tokopedia and Bukalapak are e-commerce companies, while Go-Jek started off as a ride-hailing app and is now a platform for ordering a variety of daily services. Traveloka is an online travel service.

The next wave of start-ups has focused on ancillary services such as payments, logistics and advertising technology. And while the shallowness of Indonesia s capital markets means that an IPO exit strategy is unfeasible for most start-ups, the country s unicorns are now acting as acquirers of these new businesses   further invigorating the ecosystem in the nation s capital.

President Joko Widodo s push to make Indonesia the biggest digital economy in the region has contributed to the momentum. His administration is backing an initiative to foster 1,000 start-ups by 2020 with a total valuation of $10bn, and it plays a key role in the Nexticorn programme, which brings together promising local start-ups with international investors to help with latter-stage funding.

In 2017 Mr Widodo ordered the relevant ministries to support start-ups, including by offering grants to incubators. His administration also made it easier for e-commerce companies to receive low-rate bank loans and provided simplified tax procedures for those with annual turnover below 4.8bn rupiah.

 I am pretty content with what is happening, how Jakarta is developing as a city,  said Benedicto Haryono, co-founder and chief executive of the online P2P lending service KoinWorks. Founded in 2015, it is one of the many fintech start-ups springing up in the city.

But he added that problems remain.  Hiring is still a challenge. [There is a] lack of talent, the talent pool is so small,  Mr Haryono said.  If you have money, yes you can hire, but if you have to compete with the likes of Go-Jek on hiring, it is difficult. 

This issue is  not easy to fix overnight , said Shekhar Chauhan, principal at AT Kearney. According to the consultancy, Indonesia produces only 278 engineers per 1m people each year, far behind regional peers like Malaysia or Thailand, which produce well over a thousand.

 One of the recommendations we had was facilitating, getting in more foreign talent, which is another issue in Indonesia,  Mr Chauhan said. Indonesia is wary of bringing in skilled foreign labour, as it may hamper job opportunities for its young workforce.

The combination of large amounts of money chasing start-ups without enough talent is raising some concern among investors, he added.

 That is where they might hit roadblocks,  Mr Chauhan said.

By Shotaro Tani, Nikkei staff writer

Bangkok

Number of tech start-ups

750 in Thailand

Government support

Three acts under consideration could lower barriers to start-ups, including by removing foreign ownership caps; Thailand 4.0 initiative aims to shift country to tech-oriented, knowledge economy

Education/talent pool

Mahidol University and Chulalongkorn University, both in Top 60 on QS Asia University Ranking 2018

Access to funding

Thai corporations and state-owned enterprises have launched several venture capital firms and accelerator programmes; The Stock Exchange of Thailand in May launched a crowdfunding platform for start-ups and small and midsize enterprises; foreign investment also on the rise

Transportation

Served by two international airports; well-developed transportation infrastructure within city

Office rent

1,186 baht ($36.36) per sq m per month for prime space, according to CBRE Research

The charms of Bangkok that have long attracted holiday-goers are increasingly appealing to multinational entrepreneurs wanting to set up shop in Asia. Developed infrastructure, good access to neighbouring markets, affordable prices and sunny weather are only a handful of the reasons why start-ups are sprouting in the Thai capital.

German-born Michael Cluzel, co-founder and group chief executive of restaurant booking app operator Eatigo, chose Bangkok for its headquarters and the app s debut market because of its high mobile internet penetration rate and less competitive environment compared with advanced western economies.

Eatigo offers diners time-based discounts   deals are better during off-peak hours   so that eateries can fill up their empty tables. Mr Cluzel got the idea from how aeroplanes and hotels change their rates depending on availability.

 Nobody in the world had ever done such an app for restaurants before so we needed to invent a new playbook,  Mr Cluzel told the Nikkei Asian Review.  We didn t want to do it through a looking glass in New York or San Francisco   we wanted to be in a market where we can learn, and Asia was just right. 

Bangkok was a good fit for Eatigo, with its population of 8.2m, rising middle class, few restaurant app rivals, strong dining culture and   perhaps above all   discount-loving consumers.

Mr Cluzel and his three co-founders also saw Bangkok as a springboard to other Asian markets.

 If it works here, we thought it is going to work in Manila, Kuala Lumpur and Jakarta,  he said, citing similarities such as the culture of dining out and expanding middle-income consumers.

After launching in Bangkok in 2014, the app is now in six countries, including in competitive markets like Manila, Kuala Lumpur, Mumbai, Singapore and Hong Kong   and has grabbed the leading share in many of them.

Multinational start-ups like Eatigo are not unusual in Bangkok. Japanese entrepreneur Yojiro Koshi picked Bangkok as the base for TalentEx, which offers human resource-related services, in part because he liked its mix of urban development and liveability.

 Bangkok was liveable for my family, compared to Singapore, which would be too expensive, or Jakarta, which still lacks a lot of basic infrastructure like public transportation,  he said. Bangkok also has a large base of Japanese corporations that could be his potential clients.

Rising government support is set to draw more start-ups to Thailand, especially Bangkok. The military junta has positioned start-ups as a crucial player in its  Thailand 4.0  policy, which aims to shift the nation s industries from labour-intensive to tech-oriented and knowledge-based.

One obstacle facing entrepreneurs launching a start-up in Thailand is the 49 per cent foreign ownership cap, which restricts them from receiving funds from foreign venture capital firms, and a range of business fields that are off-limits to foreigners unless they have a Thai co-founder.

But three government policies   the Thailand Startup Act, Regulatory Sandbox Act and Thai Bayh-Dole Act   are in the works that could ease some of these restrictions. A clause in the draft of the Thailand Startup Act, for example, would allow start-ups to be 100 per cent foreign-owned. The new rules are expected to be enacted before the next general election, which could come as early as February 2019.

Funding opportunities are growing, too. Thai start-ups raised at least $100m in 2017, a more than tenfold increase from 2013, according to start-up website Techsauce. Thai corporations and state-owned enterprises have launched a slew of venture capital firms and accelerator programmes in the past year or two. And in May, the Stock Exchange of Thailand launched a crowdfunding platform for start-ups and small and midsize enterprises to source funding from institutional investors and venture capital firms.

Foreign capital is also flowing in. In late 2017, Bangkok-based online fashion brand Pomelo Fashion raised $19m in a series B round led by Chinese e-commerce giant JD.com and Indonesian investment firm Provident Capital Partners. Japan s leading online fashion company Start Today (since renamed Zozo) also joined the round. It was reportedly the largest series B fundraising in Thailand at that time.

 Our investors have created so many new opportunities for our Thai company,  said David Jou, the Korean-American co-founder of Pomelo. The fast-fashion brand focuses primarily on Asian markets. Its designers are all hired locally to fit local tastes, and their price points are about half those of western fashion brands like Zara and H&M.

JD has listed Pomelo s clothes on its websites in Thailand and Indonesia and might pave the way for Pomelo to venture into the Chinese market, Mr Jou said. The fact that it is a Thai company might help: Thailand is the No. 1 destination for Chinese travellers, and its food is popular in China.

 Being a Thai company,  Mr Jou said,  is an opportunity for us. 

By Yukako Ono, Nikkei staff writer

Penang

Number of tech start-ups

More than 900 in Malaysia

Government support

Tax holidays for up to 10 years for qualifying start-ups under government programmes aimed at promoting technology companies; angel investors can receive a tax deduction of up to 500,000 ringgit ($120,000) in their third year

Education/talent pool

Malaysian Global Innovation and Creativity Centre, a federal initiative, runs its own academy offering short-term courses for school leavers and working adults; courses range from app development to augmented reality

Access to funding

State-owned Cradle Fund gives grants of between 25,000 ringgit and 800,000 ringgit. Cradle Fund, through wholly owned subsidiary Cradle Seed Ventures, invests 1m to 3m ringgit in local start-ups

Transportation

Penang International Airport connects to major cities in south-east Asia; MaGIC provides travel and accommodation allowances for participants in its Global Accelerator Programme, an exchange meeting for start-ups in the region

Office rent

200-600 ringgit ($48-144) per month for co-working space at MaGIC centers

There is no doubt that Malaysia s most famous start-up is the one that got away. Two years after Anthony Tan and Tan Hooi Ling launched the MyTeksi ride-hailing app in Malaysia, the company   by then renamed Grab   relocated its headquarters to neighbouring Singapore. Since that move in 2014, Grab has become a $10bn company and taken over Uber s south-east Asia business.

But Ashran Ghazi, who runs the Malaysian Global Innovation and Creativity Centre, or MaGIC, says he is not dwelling on the past. His agency, which was set up in 2014 to raise the country s next generation of entrepreneurs, is focused on building  awesome companies , he said. Keeping successful start-ups in Malaysia is not a primary consideration, he added.

There is no shortage of entrepreneurs in the country, many of whom are congregating in the northern island of Penang. Among them is Ai Ching Goh, who gave up her marketing job at Procter & Gamble to establish Piktochart, a visual communications app that makes it easier for nondesigners to create infographics. Founded in 2012 Piktochart boasts more than 13m registered users   about half of whom are in North America.

 We are not just a design-tool app per se but one that allows users to tell a better story through charts,  said 32-year-old Ms Goh, who has about 60 employees representing 15 nationalities.

The company s app is aimed at anyone from students to small and midsize businesses.

Penang has long been home to multinational companies, including chipmaking giant Intel, which fostered a strong engineering workforce. The state government is pushing harder to encourage entrepreneurship, a job that should be easier since the national elections in May. Penang was under opposition rule for a decade, which meant it received little aid for development from the federal government.

 It is pivotal to show that it s possible for a company based in Penang to become a global company,  said Howie Chang of aCAT, an agency launched in 2015 to promote entrepreneurship.

Among Penang s homegrown engineers is 26-year-old Jin Xi Cheong, who left Intel to start Poladrone, a drone start-up that launched in 2016 with guidance from aCat.

The company uses drones to take high-resolution photographs for technical analysis in the agriculture and oil and gas sectors. Now based near Kuala Lumpur, Poladrone counts Malaysia s leading palm oil companies like Sime Darby Plantation as its customers. Its services include measuring tree density for fertiliser forecasts and disease detection   jobs that had to be done manually before.

Plantation owners in neighbouring Indonesia, Thailand and Vietnam have knocked on Poladrone s door for solutions, said Mr Cheong, who added that he was considering expanding abroad.

Another Penang start-up is EasyParcel, an online parcel delivery operator that has tied up with more than 10 couriers, including DHL and FedEx. After four years in operation, EasyParcel has over 400,000 users in Malaysia, Indonesia, Singapore and Thailand, earning a margin from the bulk discount provided by the couriers.

Most of these start-ups share a common feature: government support of some kind, especially funding. The Cradle Fund is one of the government agencies that has been nurturing start-ups since 2003, disbursing grants of between 25,000 ringgit ($6,012) and 800,000 ringgit.

The fund, which has backed over 700 tech-based start-ups, has also become a source of venture capital. With a targeted investment size of between 1m ringgit and 3m ringgit per company, its wholly owned unit Cradle Seed Ventures takes minority equity stakes of not more than 25 per cent in companies that are involved in mobile technology, software and enterprise solutions, and hardware engineering.

To keep up with global trends, the fund is on the lookout for companies active in artificial intelligence and blockchain technology.

Dzuleira Abu Bakar, Cradle Seed Ventures chief executive, however, says the idea is not simply to copy other markets.

 I believe each country moves at its own pace given the market dynamics, readiness of talent, infrastructure and other economic variables   not so much the catch-up game but more of moving in the direction of where technology trends are headed towards. 

By CK Tan, Nikkei staff writer

Ho Chi Minh City

Number of tech start-ups

About 3,000 in Vietnam

Government support

Tax incentives, favourable land leasing terms, training programmes; government plans to launch $85m start-up fund this year; many local government-related bodies and companies have also launched funds, platforms to support start-ups

Education/talent pool

Ho Chi Minh City University of Technology, a member college of Vietnam National University Ho Chi Minh City, is known for its technology, engineering and business management programmes

Availability of funding

About 70 venture capital funds operating in Vietnam, more than two-thirds from overseas; these include CyberAgent Ventures, Incubate Fund, SoftBank Capital, Crystal Horse Investments, Asia Venture Group

Transportation

Ho Chi Minh City is served by Tan Son Nhat International Airport, Vietnam s largest airport; city s first metro line scheduled to open in 2020 to relieve heavy traffic congestion

Office rent

$50 per sq m per month for Grade A space as of second quarter, according to JLL

When ecommerce platform Sendo raised $50m this year from SoftBank and other foreign investors, it was a reflection of two trends in Vietnam: booming online sales and rising interest in one of Asia s fastest-growing economies.

As an ecommerce platform for individuals and small businesses to sell their goods, Sendo has benefited from strong annual online sales growth of 30 per cent in Vietnam in recent years. That high growth rate is expected to continue at least through 2020, according to the Vietnam E-Commerce Association.

Nguyen Dac Viet Dung, Sendo s chairman, says he plans to raise more money to keep growing.  We [will] seek one or two more rounds of fundraising in the next five years,  he told Nikkei in a recent interview.

Vietnam s start-up environment is less well-developed than regional rivals like Singapore or Indonesia, but the central government is aiming to change that. It has set up three main high-tech parks in Hanoi, Danang and Ho Chi Minh City, and it is creating incentives for the development of new high-tech businesses   including the planned launch of an $85m start-up fund this year.

Ho Chi Minh City has kicked off construction of an incubator for local tech start-ups inside the Saigon Hi-Tech Park, already home to tech giants such as Intel and Samsung. The facility will cover an area of more than 11,000 sq m, and officials say the city has spent $90m on start-up and innovation programmes there in the past two years.

Vietnam does not keep statistics on start-ups, but pan-Asia consultancy Dezan Shira & Associates says $290m was invested in 92 Vietnamese start-ups in 2017. According to tech news site Techsauce, there were about 3,000 start-ups operating in the country last year.

Investment is coming from inside and outside Vietnam. Tiki, another ecommerce platform, raised $54m from China s JD.com this year, following $17m from internet company VNG in 2016 and an undisclosed amount from CyberAgent Ventures, Seedcom and Sumitomo. Momo, an e-wallet and payments app, received $28m from Standard Chartered Private Equity and Goldman Sachs in 2016.

IDG Ventures Vietnam was the first venture capital firm to focus on technology start-ups in the country when it launched in 2004. The fund manages $100m and has invested in some 40 start-ups in the fields of technology, media and consumer industries. This year VinaCapital, an investment fund that manages $1.8bn in assets, launched the $100m VinaCapital Ventures VC fund, one of the biggest local funds for start-ups. In all, there are some 70 venture capital funds operating in Vietnam, more than two-thirds of them from overseas.

Much of the tech start-up activity in Vietnam centres on blockchain and cryptocurrency technologies   despite the fact that the country has yet to craft a regulatory framework for those industries following a series of scandals.

In April, Vietnam was at the centre of the biggest fraud in cryptocurrency history, where investors said they lost as much as $660m through scams linked to initial coin offerings. The government has since taken a hard line on the industry.

But many companies remain bullish on the opportunity for blockchain-related businesses in Vietnam   including Nami Corp. The company is developing AI and blockchain technologies for its financial investment ecosystem and products for managing financial services.

 Vietnam lacks a legal framework for crypto and related technologies, which means people are often confused when we talk about bitcoin, cryptocurrencies and blockchain,  said Giap Van Dai, the 27-year-old co-founder of Nami.  It should take four or five more years before the blockchain technique can be applied in local business communities, since it will take time for the market to accept it and for authorities to come up with related regulations. 

According to Mr Dai, the blockchain industry in Vietnam employs about 2,000 people, up from 30 in 2017. Many of them graduated overseas and gained experience developing apps, financial services and other products in developed countries, including in the US, Japan and Singapore.

 Young people are enthusiastic to learn and work in the new industry,  Mr Dai said.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors.

Single price: Based on a mid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available.

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Good day. And welcome back from the weekend, where millions of people of various ages put in some serious time in front of video games. Those masses helped "Fortnite" creator Epic Games Inc. command a nearly $15 billion valuation the subject of two stories today.

In other games, the Boston Red Sox took care of, well, business by taking Game 5 to clinch the World Series over the Los Angeles Dodgers. Which means a bit more swagger for the Massachusetts tech scene.

And now on to the news...

Today's Top Stories

Quick turnaround: David Sacks is already raising a second fund for his firm Craft Ventures, having invested most of his $350 million debut fund after a little more than a year, according to people familiar with the situation. The second fund, targeting up to $500 million, will focus on leading Series A rounds, one of the people said. It will continue to invest in the equity of blockchain companies, but will no longer buy cryptocurrencies, which underperformed. WSJ Pro's Yuliya Chernova has the exclusive.

Epic valuation: Epic Games Inc., the creator of the smash-hit videogame "Fortnite," is valued at almost $15 billion as part of a major new investment round, according to people familiar with the matter. The company Friday said it received an investment of about $1.25 billion. At least some of the investment came from sales of shares by existing holders, the people said. The new investors included KKR, Kleiner Perkins Caufield & Byers and Lightspeed Venture Partners, as well aXiomatic, which owns the competitive-videogaming organization Team Liquid. WSJ's Sarah Needleman and WSJ Pro's Katie Roof have more.

Lung deal: Galecto Biotech AB said it has raised  79 million ($90 million) to develop an alternative to the first two drugs approved for the serious lung disease idiopathic pulmonary fibrosis. Galecto's drug is inhaled, which concentrates the treatment in the lungs and limits exposure around the body. That could make it safer than existing competitors, according to the Danish company. WSJ Pro's Brian Gormley has more.

Roof's Take: On the High Price of Fun and Gaming

By Katie Roof

The competition to get a piece of the top gaming startups is fierce right now, with some investors agreeing to lofty prices to win deals.

We saw that with "Fortnite"-maker Epic Games Inc., which announced a $1.25 billion new investment round on Friday. Venture firms including Kleiner Perkins and Lightspeed invested at a nearly $15 billion valuation.

But even the most popular games can fade away as fads and get into trouble.

So why all the big bets on gaming?

Ethan Kurzweil, a partner at Bessemer and early investor in game-streaming platform Twitch before it sold to Amazon.com Inc., says investor skepticism may have actually helped some businesses.

More in Roof's Take.

Exits

Shares of Gamida Cell Ltd. closed Friday at $8.44 apiece, up 5.5% from their initial public offering price. The Jerusalem-based developer of cell therapies sold 6.25 million shares at $8 apiece in the IPO.

Maven Ventures hopes to raise up to $50 million for its third fund, according to a regulatory filing. The Palo Alto, Calif.-based firm provides seed investments to consumer software startups. Portfolio companies include autonomous shopping startup Zyp, toy rental service Pley and videoconferencing tech provider Zoom.

New Money

Conversica Inc., a Foster City, Calif.-based provider of conversational artificial intelligence for businesses, has secured $31 million in Series C funding led by Providence Strategic Growth Capital Partners. New investors CIBC and Savano Capital also participated, along with previous backers Toba Capital and Kennet Partners. "https://www.conversica.com"

Innovusion, a Los Altos, Calif.-based developer of Lidar technology for autonomous vehicles, has raised $30 million in Series A funding. Investors including NIO Capital, Eight Roads Ventures and F-Prime Capital Partners participated. "https://www.innovusion.com"

Infoworks.io, a Palo Alto, Calif.-based big-data automation startup, has completed a $25 million Series C round led by New Enterprise Associates. Nexus Venture Partners and Centerview Capital Technology also invested. "https://www.infoworks.io"

Coople, a London-based on-demand staffing platform, has raised a $21 million funding round. One Peak Partners, Goldman Sachs Private Capital and Harbert European Growth Capital were the investors. In addition to the financing, the company has appointed founder Viktor Calabr to the role of chairman, and has promoted Rinaldo Olivari, managing director for the U.K., to group CEO. Dayna Grayson of NEA and Michael Oleshansky of PSP will join the company's board. "https://www.wirewheel.io"

Industry News

The tide is suddenly turning against some of China's most valuable technology startups. In recent weeks, several Chinese tech unicorns have struggled to meet fundraising targets, according to bankers and investors. A few have delayed plans for IPOs, while some investors have lowered their estimates of the worth of one of China's top startups. Read more.

Chinese banks start scanning borrowers  facial ticks

Oliver Ralph, Don Weinland and Martin Arnold 
647 words
28 October 2018
21:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Ping An technology designed to help vet potentially fraudulent borrowers

Chinese banks have started using micro-expression technology in an effort to spot the early signs of fraud in customers  facial movements.

The banks are worried that some potential customers may be lying about the uses of loans, so they have developed systems that use smartphone cameras to detect minute facial expressions and indicate when a user is being economical with the truth.

Chinese banks are among the first in the world to use the technology commercially. Ping An, the financial services conglomerate, has developed the technology and is using it in its lending businesses. Western groups have so far shied away from the technology because of concerns about its reliability and the ethics of using it.

Ping An s technology can identify 54 brief, involuntary micro-expressions, which the face often creates before the brain has a chance to control facial movements. They include eyeballs moving back and forth and rapid blinking, and often take place in only 1/15 to 1/25 of a second.

Lee Yuansiong, one of Ping An s deputy chief executives, said:  We use micro expression recognition technology to review loan applications. It captures subtle changes in customers  facial expressions which help to identify and warn against fraud risks. 

He added:  We ve reduced credit losses by 60 per cent using this technology. It is more accurate than other approaches to fraud detection. 

The company does not use micro-expressions alone to decide whether or not to lend money. However, if the technology spots something amiss, the client will be flagged for extra investigation. Ping An is not the only company experimenting with the technology.

Kee Sun-Tuan, chief information officer in China at Standard Chartered, said:  Micro-expression technology acts like a lie-detector, using the camera in a phone to check people s responses to questions and scan their facial movements. 

One of the advantages of the system is that it allows banks to remotely assess lending risk. That means they can start lending in places where they do not necessarily have a physical presence.

That is not so much of an issue for Ping An, which has 1.4m insurance agents across China. But StanChart s Chinese network has only slightly more than 100 outlets.

 We are testing this technology which, as one of the tools for due diligence, could potentially allow us to serve a wider group of clients through remote lending, without the need for an extensive physical branch network,  added Mr Kee.

Not everyone is a fan of the new technology though. Husayn Kassai, co-founder of facial recognition company Onfido, said academic studies had  not always yielded consistent results  on whether micro-expression systems were a reliable indicator of deception.

 Besides, even if the feasibility is proven through research, there are serious ethical questions around such a use case,  said Mr Kassai.  For instance, such a system would probably need to be used without the user s awareness in order to capture natural micro-expressions which raises questions around a user s informed consent. 

There are questions over the consequences of turning down a loan application on the basis of a false positive from the technology, he said, adding that it would be hard to explain under Europe s new general data protection regulation.

Ping An has made investment in technology a priority, putting 1 per cent of its annual revenues into research and development. That adds up to Rmb50bn ($7.2bn) over the past decade. The group employs 23,000 R&D personnel and 500 big data experts.

 A lot of data is shared within Ping An, with the customers  permission,  said Mr Lee.  We use big data to guide our product development, service strategy, underwriting and risk selection.

China sees explosive growth of billionaires

Chris Flood 
626 words
26 October 2018
11:08
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Rich Chinese entrepreneurs are joining the global billionaires club at the rate of two a week, driving a record $1.4tn annual increase in the collective wealth of the world s most exclusive tribe to an all-time high of $8.9tn.

Just 30 years after China allowed private enterprise, the country s billionaires now number 373 and account for almost one in five of the global total of 2,158, according to an annual survey compiled by UBS, the world s largest wealth manager, and PwC, the professional services provider.

 China has overtaken the US as the place where exceptional wealth is created at the fastest rate. China s vast population, technology innovation and productivity growth, combined with government support, are providing unprecedented opportunities for individuals to build businesses,  said Josef Stadler, head of ultra high net worth at UBS s wealth management unit.

The explosive growth of the Chinese billionaire class is contributing to rising inequality across the country, an issue of mounting concern to policymakers in Beijing.

The 19th national congress of the Communist party, held in October 2017, identified the building of a  moderately prosperous society  as a goal, implying a recognition of the need to spread the benefits of China s growing economic wealth more fairly.

Virtually all of China s billionaires, who saw their combined wealth surge 39 per cent last year to $1.2tn, have made their own fortunes even though many of the early successful entrepreneurs had little formal education.

Technology entrepreneurs building businesses in the internet, ecommerce, biotechnology and artificial intelligence sectors are now contributing a growing number to the new entrants to China s billionaires cohort.

Stock market flotations have helped the founders of companies to crystallise huge gains, with many of the initial public offerings on the country s stock markets in 2017 commanding high valuations.

Wang Jian, a former research fellow at the University of Washington in Seattle, became an overnight billionaire when BGI Genomics floated on the Shenzhen exchange in July 2017. Mr Wang owns a 32.5 per cent stake in BGI, which saw its market capitalisation surge from $1.2bn at the time of the IPO to a peak of about $16bn in November before dropping to about $2.9bn this week.

A total of 631 new companies floated on stock exchanges across the greater China region in 2017, raising a total of $52bn, according to PwC.

The explosive growth of the Chinese billionaires class is contributing to rising inequality across the country, an issue of mounting concern to policymakers in Beijing.

The most recent national congress of the ruling Communist party, held in October 2017, identified the building of a "moderately prosperous society" as a goal, implying a recognition of the need to spread the benefits of China's growing economic wealth more fairly.

In the US, membership of the billionaires club reached 585 after gaining 22 net new joiners, helping to swell their combined wealth by 12 per cent to $3.1tn.

Mr Stadler said growth in the US was far slower than five years ago, possibly due in part to pressures on hedge fund managers where both fees and performance have deteriorated.

 We are also witnessing the rise of  stealth wealth  where the tendency to keep businesses in private ownership for longer before a listing on the public market means that wealth creation is under-reported in the US and also in Europe,  said Mr Stadler.

Seventeen new billionaires were minted in Europe, taking its ultra-rich population to 414. The combined wealth of Europe s billionaires rose 19 per cent to $1.9tn, partly reflecting the substantial appreciation in the value of the euro against the dollar last year.

WSJ Pro
Kang's Take: Capitalizing on Merger Magic

By Jaewon Kang 
448 words
25 October 2018
22:10
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Private equity is increasingly hoping that one plus one will equal three.

More private-equity firms are buying two midsize companies simultaneously with the goal of combining them, a tactic they hope will generate stronger returns as they continue to face rising valuations and a shortage of assets.

Indeed, the strategy has become more common for firms in the past few years. Thoma Bravo, for instance, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/thoma-bravo-to-merge-runzheimer-motus-1516220498?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1" mileage-tracking software makers Runzheimer International Ltd. and Motus LLC earlier this year. In 2017, Centerbridge Partners"https://www.wsj.com/articles/clearlake-sells-syncsort-and-vision-solutions-to-centerbridge-partners-1499338800?mod=article_inline"analytics companies Syncsort Inc. and Vision Solutions Inc. from Clearlake Capital Group. Bain Capital, meanwhile, combined utility businesses Big Tex Trailers Inc. and American Trailer Works Inc. the year before that.

While firms have occasionally pursued this combination strategy, investors particularly larger-cap sponsors increasingly are thinking about the benefits of doing such combination deals, said Mike Lindemann, a managing director at midmarket-focused investment bank Robert W. Baird & Co.

"Valuations have certainly gone up in the last couple of years. There's a dearth of targets and investments for larger sponsors within their core size range," said Mr. Lindemann, who typically advises on transactions ranging from $100 million to $3 billion.

Upper midmarket investors, for instance, will consider buying the No. 2 and No. 3 players in a niche category of a broader industry where there tends to be less regulatory risk, Mr. Lindemann said.

Firms are betting that adding two smaller businesses won't only result in an entity that is just as big as or bigger than the No. 1 player, but also create synergies with their existing infrastructure and customers. By combining peers early on, private-equity firms also are hoping they'll have enough time to work through any operational kinks and ultimately generate higher returns.

Such transactions are taking place as private-equity firms go lower in the market to deploy capital. In the continuing sellers' market, investors are finding a flurry of similar businesses testing the water. This wave of combo deals is likely to continue, particularly against the backdrop of record-high amounts of capital being raised by private equity.

Still, the marriage of two peers often requires much more work than do add-on acquisitions. Investors must be ready to roll up their sleeves to cut costs and further build the combined entity's presence, as they search for potential avenues of growth. Certainly, the benefits of such deals could be huge, but the risks could be even bigger making it hard to get a positive number out of one plus one.

Write to Jaewon Kang at "mailto:jaewon.kang@wsj.com"

Pro Private Markets
Intel's Venture Arm Lays Off One Quarter of Investment Team; Firm seeks to make a smaller number of larger deals more closely related to Intel's core business

By Tomio Geron 
671 words
25 October 2018
04:43
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Intel Capital, the venture arm of semiconductor giant Intel Corp., has laid off one quarter of its 60-person investment team, as the firm moves to making a smaller number of larger deals more closely related to its parent company's core business.

The shift reflects the challenges corporate venture arms of large companies face in keeping up with rapid changes in the venture industry as corporate priorities shift over time.

Intel Capital, with active investments in more than 300 companies, has been one of Silicon Valley's most prolific corporate venture firms, investing broadly in areas including software, internet, drones, artificial intelligence, cybersecurity and autonomous vehicles.

The firm, however, has been moving over the past few years toward backing a smaller number of deals with larger checks, and that has led to a decision in August to reduce staffing.

Wendell Brooks, president of Intel Capital, said it didn't make sense to have 60 investment professionals aiming to make 30 deals a year, which is about half the number of deals the firm did annually in the years before he was put in charge in 2015. He added that the decision was "gut-wrenching" but was necessary to support the new focus and to add the most value for its portfolio companies.

"There's deal creep where you want to do more than 30 deals a year," Mr. Brooks said. "It finally dawned on me that we had a structural problem."

So far this year, Intel Capital has invested in 31 new companies and 41 follow-on deals, investing a total of $252 million, according to the company.

The changes come as the firm's corporate parent is undergoing a search for a new chief executive following "https://www.wsj.com/articles/intel-ceo-brian-krzanich-resigns-after-violating-company-policy-1529586884"in June. Mr. Brooks said his unit's new strategy has the support of top Intel Corp. management.

As part of the changes, Mr. Brooks said he is reinstating a compensation policy resembling the way traditional venture firms pay its investors carried interest, or a percentage of the profits, when a portfolio company is sold or goes public. Intel did away with carry for its investment staff shortly before he became president of Intel Capital, he said.

The firm also will no longer do seed-stage deals, but will still do venture deals at the Series A, B, and C stages with the aim of investing about $400 million a year.

Since Mr. Brooks joined Intel Capital, the firm also has moved to focus more on strategic investments in startups that are directly relevant to Intel's chip business rather than its previously broader mix of deals.

Mr. Brooks said the firm will still invest across a variety of areas including artificial intelligence, cloud computing and the Internet of Things, but it intends to stop investing in sectors such as e-commerce and related areas.

"We got together in August and very thoroughly decided which domains we want to focus on and which we can add value to relative to the rest of Intel's domain expertise," Mr. Brooks said.

One of the investors departing is Christine Herron, an early-stage investor who was also co-lead of Intel Capital's Diversity Initiative, formerly known as the Diversity Fund, a program that backs founders who are women or minorities, as well as those who are lesbian, gay, bisexual and transgender, disabled or veterans. Ms. Herron, who had been at the firm eight years, declined to comment. Mr. Brooks said Intel Capital will continue operating the Diversity Initiative program.

Since 1991, Intel Capital has invested more than $12 billion in more than 1,500 companies globally.

Write to Tomio Geron at "mailto:tomio.geron@wsj.com"

Stefania Palma in Sydney 
414 words

Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

New product expected to provide valuable insight into south-east Asian consumers

Grab, the south-east Asian ride-hailing company, has partnered with Mastercard to launch prepaid cards, in a move that will give both parties valuable consumer insight in one of the world s most financially excluded regions.

The two companies aim to launch both virtual and physical versions of the prepaid cards in the first half of 2019, starting in Singapore, Grab s home market, and the Philippines, with the intention of rolling out the service across the region.

The cards will give Grab and Mastercard significant insight into consumer trends in a region where 200m people are expected to enter the middle class by 2030.

Grab, which was set up in 2012 and is currently valued at $11bn, is moving aggressively into sectors beyond ride-hailing, such as healthcare, food delivery and mobile payments, helping to cement its dominant position in the region at a time when rivals, including Indonesia s Go-Jek, accelerate their own expansions.

Reuben Lai, senior managing director at Grab s financial arm, said the new product would help Grab create products and services tailored to the needs of consumers and merchants, and price them effectively.

Rama Sridhar, executive vice-president for digital and emerging partnerships and new payment flows at Mastercard, said pairing Grab s existing insight into consumer behaviour with the US company s data analytics and experience with financial institutions would help  create lending products, which is what most regulators are hoping for across south-east Asia .

Partnering with Grab gives Mastercard access to a region where credit card use has historically been low. According to Boston Consulting Group, there are 177 credit cards for every 100 people in the US, compared with 29 per 100 in Thailand and 5 per 100 in Vietnam.

 It helps us leverage Grab as a partner to tap into a huge demographic that was perhaps underserved until now,  said Ms Sridhar.

However, BCG said last month that in south-east Asia, where small businesses tend to prefer cash on delivery over credit cards, consumers are likely to  leap directly from cash to digital payments, skipping credit and debit cards .

Asked who Grab s competitors would be in prepaid cards, Mr Lai said:  Seventy per cent [of south-east Asians] are unbanked. Ninety per cent don t have a card. Who is our competitor? Cash or digital wallets?

Investors Inject $46 Million More Into Biotech Startup XtalPi; China Life, Google and Sequoia are backing a company that aims to help drug developers spot promising compounds

RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Biotech startup XtalPi Inc. has closed its second financing this year, nabbing $46 million to boost its ability to help drugmakers be more efficient.

XtalPi uses artificial intelligence, quantum physics and cloud computing to aid pharmaceutical researchers. The company, which disclosed a $15 million Series B round in January, says it now has raised a total of $66 million including this Series B-1 extension.

XtalPi says it can help pharmaceutical scientists predict which drug compounds have desirable qualities. This helps them make better decisions about which to develop, said spokeswoman Ruyu Wang.

The company already can predict certain physical and chemical characteristics of drug molecules. It expects to gain the ability to predict more of these characteristics with the help of this financing, according to Ms. Wang.

XtalPi, based in Cambridge, Mass., in May said it had joined with Pfizer Inc. to develop a software platform for molecular modeling of small-molecule drugs. XtalPi hasn't revealed names of other drugmakers it is working with, Ms. Wang said.

China Life Healthcare Fund led this new financing, which also included SIG and Yael Capital. Investors in the original Series B included Google, Sequoia China and Tencent.

Silicon Roundabout trumps Silicon Valley

Hannah Murphy 
1,148 words
23 October 2018
09:36
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

In fintech at least, Shoreditch is stealing a march over San Francisco

Silicon Valley has long dwarfed London s Silicon Roundabout as the number one global tech hub. But new evidence shows that in the fintech space at least, Shoreditch is stealing a march over San Fran.

UK newbies   ie firms set up after 2005   now account for an  eye popping  63 per cent of the UK'sfinancial servicescompanies and 14 per cent of the region's $206bn banking and payments revenues, according to a new report by consultancy Accenture.

The ascendancy of the British upstarts far outstrips their progress in the US, where firms created after 2005 make up just 19 per cent of the financial services market and have won just 3.5 per cent of the $1.04tn pool of banking and payments revenues.

Accenture, which analysed more than 20,000 banking and payments companies globally for the report, cites several explanations for why start-ups are having a better time of it in the UK.

Firstly, post-crisis regulations in the US lifted the bar for new entrants seeking a banking licence there   to the benefit of large incumbents. The Accenture analysis found that more than half of current accounts opened in the US in 2017 were captured by the biggest three banks, who are all spending big on digital development.

Meanwhile US regional banks are focused on  cost reduction and struggling to grow their balance sheets . This means they have ceded some ground to up-and-coming groups such as Stripe, the San Francisco-based private payments company that was valued at a plump $20bn after its latest investment round.

In the UK, the regulatory landscape is helping the smallest players take on the old guard. In 2010, the UK s Financial Conduct Authority granted Metro a full banking licence   marking the first new UKbank to be licensed in 150 years. Now 15 fintechs, including Monzo, Atom Bank and Starling, hold full banking licences.

Industry insiders say broader support from regulators has also been keyin nurturing fintechs. TheFCA launched a  sandbox in 2016that gives companies temporary approval to try out new products, reducing the time and cost of getting them to market, and allowing them to raise funding more easily.

Close to 100 firms have been accepted into the sandbox since its birth. In the US, regulators have been more cautious, with the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau only announcing plans for a sandbox in July this year, for example.

The FCA has alsopushed aheadwith the early adoption of the EU s  open banking  rules, which force big lenders to open up access to client data for fintechs, where permission has been granted.

Tristan Thomas, Monzo s head of marketing and community, said the rules have already meant some  are starting to compete [even] without a banking licence .

These include fintechs that are now  offering services such as budgeting and acting as a central hub for your services, by pulling together lots of bank accounts into one view , he said. The UK's Budexplicitly takes advantage of the open banking rules to deliver a service itdescribesas  part fintech app store, part financial marketplace, part search engine . Another venture,Emma, uses artificial intelligence technology to help you manage your spending.

Momentum in the space brings risks, of course. Let s not forget that several months ago, fast-growing pre-paid card operator Revolutrevealedthat it had discovered a spate of suspected money laundering on its systems and had to contact authorities, prompting concerns over its compliance processes.

Still,the future remains fairly bright and there is bounty on the horizons, including a775m pot of cash that the Royal Bank of Scotland is set to invest into the small banking sector next February, under orders from European regulators.

A pessimist could liken this to the dying man's rally, the final EU boost for UK fintechs before the hammer comes down on Brexit in March and untold disasters erupt. Fortunately, there are more optimists than pessimists in the fintech world.

Further fintech fascination

RIP ICOs?Remember Initial Coin Offerings (ICOs)? They were all the rage in the first quarter, when you could scarcely move (at a fintech conference at least) without running into someone who was suitably smug about his or her share of the $6.9bn that was raised from issuing coinin the first three months of the year. April and May were pretty good months too, with another $3.7bn raised. Since then, not so much. In the following four months   ie June through September   less than $1.7bn was raised in ICO money, according to Token Data. Alphaville's Jemima Kelly explains how crytpo iskilling itself.

Peer-to-painChina s peer-to-peer lenders have warned their numbers will fall from around 1,500 to as few as 50 over the next year as regulators crack down on debt and risk in the $120bn industry. The clampdown has already prompted a wave of defaults, leading to protests from retail investors, while many executives in the industry have been barred by police from leaving the country. Separately, British peer-to-peer property lender Lendy appears to have come unstuck and appealed to the FCA for help, after almost two-thirds of its borrowers failed to repay their loans on time. More here.

Banking on blockchainFurther evidence that after years of talking about blockchain, big banks are finallydoing blockchain. NatWest, a subsidiary of the Royal Bank of Scotland, last week becamethe first bankto sign up toa new blockchain-based platform that aims to dramatically streamline the process around the3.5tn a year global syndicated loans market. Fusion LenderComm, whichwas developed by Finastra,R3and a consortium of banks, is expected to add others to its platform shortly.The news comes just weeks after JPMorgan Chase and its partners announced theexpansionof their blockchain-based interbank paymentsplatform, which is now banks' biggest live blockchain project.
