U.S.  Unbanked' Population Continues to Fall; More households have bank accounts thanks to strong economy, according to 2017 FDIC survey

By Allison Prang and Yuka Hayashi 
564 words
23 October 2018
20:02
WSJ Pro Central Banking
RSTPROCB
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

The number of U.S. households without a bank account fell to 6.5% in 2017, according to a "https://www.fdic.gov/householdsurvey/2017/2017report.pdf", as the improved economy helped bring mainstream banking services to more people.

The Federal Deposit Insurance Corp. said Tuesday the number of "unbanked" households reached its lowest level last year since the regulator began the biennial survey in 2009. The share of households without an account at a federally insured financial institution was down half a percentage point from 7% in 2015, which the FDIC said "can be explained almost entirely by improvements in the socioeconomic circumstances of U.S. households."

In 2017, the U.S. had 8.4 million unbanked households, or about 14.1 million adults, though the number has been falling since 2011. That year, 8.2% of U.S. households were unbanked.

Lower-income households and households that are less educated have higher rates of being unbanked and underbanked, the FDIC said. The regulator defines someone who is underbanked as a person who has an account at an insured firm but also uses financial services outside the traditional banking system, such as payday loans or check-cashing services.

In 2017, 18.7% of U.S. households were considered underbanked, the FDIC said, down from 19.9% in 2015.

The latest survey highlighted a rapid change in how households accessed banking services in the digital age. The proportion of households with bank accounts that used mobile banking in the previous 12 months soared to 40.4% in 2017, nearly double the level in 2013. Online banking was used by 63% of households.

Among those who used mobile banking, 35% checked balances or transactions, and 26.5% paid bills. Gaining in popularity are relatively new functions such as depositing checks electronically, which was embraced by 18% of households with bank accounts, up from 5.6% in 2013.

Despite consumers' expanded access to banking, one in five households, or 22.7 million, didn't use mainstream credit such as credit cards and mortgages in the prior year, which may have left them without a credit score. Meanwhile, 22.1% of households turned to nonbank firms providing such services as check cashing, remittances and payday loans, typically charging much higher fees than banks.

The FDIC estimates about a fifth of households likely have little or no credit history. Black and Hispanic households, or those headed by a person with disability, account for large chunks of this group. Fair Isaac Corp., the provider of the widely used FICO credit score, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/want-a-higher-credit-score-soon-your-cash-could-help-1540123200"next year that factors in how consumers manage money in their bank accounts.

"There are a number of firms who are looking to innovate in this space, and to use new data, new model and technique," said Keith Ernst, associate director for consumer research and examination analytics at the FDIC. "We do think that investments in that sort of technology have the potential to open up access to credit for more and more households."

Battle for Mexico's Oro Negro Heats Up as Creditors Attempt to Seize Oil Rigs; Company faces standoff with bondholders on oil rig

By Robbie Whelan and Tom Corrigan 
713 words
23 October 2018
02:07
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2018 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The battle for control over bankrupt Mexican oil-drilling company Oro Negro turned ugly over the weekend, as attorneys for the company's creditors, escorted by Mexican federal police officers, flew in helicopters to Oro Negro's five drilling rigs in the Gulf of Mexico and attempted to seize control of them, setting up a tense standoff that continued Monday.

On Friday, a criminal court judge in Mexico City ruled the rigs must be handed over to the bondholders.

Mexico City prosecutors have been investigating Oro Negro, an embattled oil-services firm led Gonzalo Gil, the son of Mexico's former finance minister, for fraud, according to disclosures Oro Negro made in a New York bankruptcy court last week.

Ricardo Contreras, a lawyer representing the creditors group, on Sunday flew out over the shallow waters of the Gulf of Mexico with the police officers, attempting to enforce the judge's order. He managed to land on just one of the drilling platforms, known as Decus, after workers placed obstacles on the heliports of the others, according to videos of the incident and both sides' accounts.

"A criminal judge ordered restitution of the rigs and ordered federal authorities to assist in those orders. It's that simple," said Paul Leand, a New York investor who heads the ad hoc committee of Oro Negro bondholders, an oversight body. "At the end of the day, we have no interest in holding on to these rigs. What we're interested in is protecting and securing our collateral."

The dust-up is just the latest chapter in a yearslong fight between Oro Negro, its creditors and Petrleos Mexicanos, or Pemex, Mexico's massive state oil company, but illustrates how the 2014 collapse in oil prices continues to reverberate even today for some investors.

On the eve of Mexico's historic oil reform, which was signed into law in 2013, Oro Negro raised nearly $1 billion in debt and hundreds of millions in equities, leased five drilling rigs from Singapore, and rushed to join a handful of oil-services companies offering to lease maritime oil platforms known as jackup rigs to Pemex to help the oil giant increase crude oil production.

But the collapse in crude prices prompted Pemex to pull back, slashing the day rates it paid to contractors. Soon, Oro Negro couldn't make debt payments and filed for bankruptcy protection in Mexico last year.

As the situation began to sour, all three parties began exchanging legal broadsides. This year, Oro Negro filed a complaint under the North American Free Trade Agreement, alleging Pemex colluded with bondholders to put the driller out of business and hand over its assets to a competitor that had received preferential treatment through the downturn. Pemex and the bondholders deny any wrongdoing.

"The oil-field services sector has taken a major beating over the course of the downturn in prices, and specifically the offshore market has had a tough time," said Pablo Medina, an Houston-based analyst with research firm Welligence Energy Analytics. "A lot of the investments in that space were very ill-timed. Pemex's reduction of its drilling activities took a lot of companies like Oro Negro by surprise."

Oro Negro sought and won "https://www.wsj.com/articles/mexicos-oro-negro-files-for-u-s-bankruptcy-court-protection-1524520211?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=3"in the U.S. in April, defeating an objection from bondholders. Chapter 15 acts much like chapter 11, halting lawsuits and blocking creditors from seizing assets governed by U.S. law.

On Monday, Oro Negro filed a lawsuit asking Judge Shelley Chapman, the New York bankruptcy judge overseeing the Oro Negro case, for a cease-and-desist order preventing bondholders from taking over or otherwise interfering with the rigs. The lawsuit also is seeking payment for damages.

What happens next is unclear. As of Monday afternoon, Mr. Contreras, the lawyer for the bondholders, as well another individual, remained on the Decus oil rig, without contact with the company, and additional helicopters have been blocked from landing, according to the bondholder group.

Cyber Daily: Talk of  Algorithmic Warfare' in New York Harbor; Cyber Daily: Talk of  Algorithmic Warfare' in New York Harbor

1,495 words
23 October 2018

WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Corporate, Military Leaders Look to AI to Stay Ahead of Hackers

By Kim S. Nash

ABOARD THE HMS QUEEN ELIZABETH -- Leaders of the U.K. Royal Navy and U.S. Navy agreed Monday to cooperate to develop cybersecurity and artificial intelligence capabilities. AI is steadily transforming cybersecurity, yet there are no established rules for how to apply AI or how to explain the decisions it makes, sometimes with humans removed from the process, military and corporate leaders warn.

The agreement is intended to bolster defense technology as cybersecurity threats intensify, said Commodore Ian Annett, assistant chief of staff for information warfare and CIO of the Royal Navy, who spoke at the Atlantic Future Forum on Monday aboard the HMS Queen Elizabeth, a Royal Navy aircraft carrier anchored off New York Harbor.

Companies and military organizations should expect adversaries, including nation-states, to use AI technology against them, said Rear Adm. David Hahn, chief of Naval Research for the U.S. Navy, speaking at the forum.

Defensive and offensive cybersecurity tools to use aboard and launch from ships provide military leaders with more options to deal with adversaries, Rear Adm. Hahn said. "This ship is the platform."

State of the art vessel

The HMS Queen Elizabeth, which is the biggest ship in the Royal Navy, was built to accommodate the F-35B stealth fighter jet from Lockheed Martin Corp. The ship cost about 3 billion, or about $3.9 billion.

The vessel can carry up to 40 aircraft and was designed in part to store and analyze data for cyberwarfare efforts at sea, according to the Royal Navy. It is expected to enter service in 2020.

"The most important thing is we recognize we have to be a first mover," Rear Adm. Hahn said. "We have to move faster than competitors."

AI tools can help companies prepare for cyberattacks by, for instance, automating parts of exercises that repeatedly test corporate and military defenses. AI also can play a role in responding to attacks underway, when malicious code is spreading in a network faster than a network administrator or other staff member can stop it. Companies have developed processes and tools for detecting and responding to cybersecurity incidents. The next step is to anticipate them, said Sean Plankey, cyber intelligence advisor at BP America Inc.

Cracking Open the Algorithms

Attack simulations that use AI to analyze information about known attacks can help corporations and governments understand where and how hackers will strike, Mr. Plankey said in an interview on board the Queen Elizabeth. "You want to get in front with intelligence to characterize adversaries," he said. BP and Lockheed sponsored the Atlantic Future Forum with Amazon Web Services, BAE Systems PLC, BlackRock Inc., C5 Capital, CQS, Ineos and Raytheon Co.

Today, transparency isn't a given in military systems, said Chris C. Demchak, Rear Adm. Grace M. Hopper Professor of Cyber Security and director of the Cyber and Innovation Policy Institute at the U.S. Naval War College.

Defense contractors historically have kept much of their proprietary technology secret but that approach won't work as AI is woven through cyberattacks and the military will have to adjust algorithms to respond to changes in tactics from attackers, Dr. Demchak said at the forum.

"You get [AI systems] in black boxes and you're not allowed to break them open," she said. "If you don't have the ability to open them, you don't have the ability to correct for what the adversaries are doing."

********************************

Automation, AI Can Help Business Leaders Before and After Cyber Incidents

By Catherine Stupp

NEW YORK -- More than one year after FedEx Corp. was hit by the NotPetya attack, the company's chief information security officer sees the need for businesses to use automation when they respond to big, fast-spreading attacks.

Some of FedEx's computer systems shut down around the world when they were infected by the malware that spread across Europe in June 2017.

The attack, which cost FedEx $400 million in recovery expenses, taught the company's security team that automated cyber defense systems could detect fast-moving viruses much more quickly than humans are able to do.

"Use incidence response, maybe even use some of the advanced techniques such as machine learning and automated measures because human beings cannot respond in a matter of minutes if this kind of volume spreads," FedEx's Gene Sun said Monday, speaking during a panel discussion at the ONUG conference, an industry gathering focused on open infrastructure and standards.

"We always measure ourselves on the mean time to detect and mean time to respond. In those kind of events, minutes and seconds matter," he added.

The NotPetya malware hit Ukrainian accounting software company MeDoc and then moved into FedEx's computer systems, according to Mr. Sun.

"Tens of thousands of machines got bricked, it was within a few minutes worldwide," Mr. Sun said.

Letting code do human work

Some companies also look to artificial intelligence as a way to bolster the preventive measures they already use by making them faster and more efficient.

James Routh, chief security officer at Aetna Inc., said companies could benefit from automating red-teaming exercises, which simulate cyber attacks to test employee readiness but can be time-consuming for security teams to run.

"That's where a lot of the labor intensity comes from. So if you can do that, you can run red teams rapidly. If you do them quarterly, you can do them weekly. There's a lot of newer technology that allows us to create a lot of synthetic incidents," Mr. Routh said, speaking at the ONUG conference.

"The faster you can do that cycle and the more automation you can apply to it, the more often you can do it," he added.

Simulations can provide clear data to help make decisions about cybersecurity approaches, said Richard Warner, general manager of U.S. federal business at Improbable Worlds Ltd.

Improbable, a startup based in London, supplies simulation technology to game developers and companies and wants to work with U.S. defense contractors to simulate cybersecurity exercises for the military. The company, backed in part by SoftBank Group Corp., is a U.K. unicorn, valued at more than $1 billion.

"Too often, we make decisions without evidence and fail to understand the interconnectedness of systems," he said.

A British Court ruled Monday that retailer Wm Morrison Supermarkets PLC, known as Morrisons, is liable for a data breach in which employees' personal data was posted online by a disgruntled staffer, the Associated Press reports. The chain said it would appeal to the Supreme Court.

Super Micro to review motherboards for spy chips. One of the largest suppliers of server motherboards said on Monday that it would review its hardware for malicious chips following allegations made in a recent Bloomberg Businessweek article, Reuters reports. According to the Bloomberg report, the Chinese government installed small chips into motherboards made by Super Micro Computer Inc., allowing Beijing to spy on a variety of large American technology firms, many of whom denied the allegations. "Despite the lack of any proof that a malicious hardware chip exists, we are undertaking a complicated and time-consuming review to further address the article," Super Micro wrote in a letter to customers.

CEO Tim Cook, CNBC reports. Mr. Jassy wrote in a tweet Monday that the Bloomberg report, which alleged that Super Micro hardware used by Apple and AWS was compromised by the Chinese government, offered no proof and was poorly reported. "Reporters got played or took liberties. Bloomberg should retract," he said.

The government commission requested that Facebook fully communicate security issues to users, increase surveillance of third-party applications on the social network and inform regulators of changes in security safeguards. The request comes weeks after a security breach at Facebook compromised the personal information of about 29 million people.

Gene-editing technology could potentially transform everything from human health to agriculture. First, however, scientists need help to make that happen.

Synthego Corp. is among several startups angling to be the go-to supplier of tools needed in Crispr-based experiments. Founded in 2012 by former SpaceX engineers, the company provides thousands of researchers with software and molecular materials like enzymes and synthetic RNA.

Now, with $110 million in new funding, the company plans to launch products that get researchers out of the lab and into clinical testing. Founders Fund led the round, which brings the Redwood City, Calif.-based company's total raised to date to $160 million. Existing investors 8VC and Menlo Ventures also contributed.

"A lot of gene-editing and cell therapies are moving into the clinical phase, and we want to be their enabler," said co-founder and Chief Executive Paul Dabrowski.

Synthego's platform was designed to help researchers cut their costs and speed up their work through automation and machine learning.

Clients from agriculture to biopharmaceuticals can use the platform to identify which cell lines or enzymes are needed for a particular experiment, then order synthetically engineered versions of those products.

Since launching its first products in 2016, Synthego said it has acquired thousands of customers including large corporations, startups and academic institutions. Thus far, its products are only approved for use in the research phase. Now, the company plans to build out a certified facility and product portfolio cleared for use in clinical studies.

To get to that point, Mr. Dabrowski said Synthego is working closely with its clinical partners to develop the next generation of the platform and product line. Stanford professor and physician Dr. Matthew Porteus, a cell therapy expert who is the scientific co-founder of Crispr Therapeutics AG, has joined Synthego's board.

Clearlake Buying Software Provider Symplr From CapStreet, Pamlico; Deal values symplr at $500 million

By Laura Cooper

23 October 2018
02:00
WSJ Pro Private Equity

English
Copyright  2018, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Clearlake Capital Group agreed to acquire Vendor Credentialing Service LLC, a provider of health-care credentialing software that does business as symplr.

The deal values the company at approximately $550 million, said a person familiar with the situation.

The transaction is an exit for existing investors CapStreet Group, which invested in symplr in 2012, and Pamlico Capital, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/DJFLBO0120151123ebbnksnzc?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=1" the company in 2015.

The acquisition by Clearlake was the result of an auction process run by William Blair & Co., which worked with symplr and its backers on the sale.

Symplr's software-as-a-service platform is focused on health-care governance, and risk-and-compliance services, including provider management, credentialing and payer enrollment. The Houston company provides its services to more than 1,500 customers, including physicians, hospitals and health-care vendors.

Another person familiar with the deal said the company has had strong organic growth, with more than 20% top line revenue growth year over year.

Clearlake is likely to use symplr as a platform for acquisitions, targeting companies in the credentialing space as well as those in adjacent areas such as patient safety and analytics, the person said.

Santa Monica, Calif.-based Clearlake makes investments in companies in the software and technology, energy and industrials, and food and consumer-services sectors. The firm in March said it raised more than $3.6 billion for "https://www.wsj.com/articles/clearlake-raises-over-3-6-billion-for-its-latest-fund-1521194401?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=2".

Earlier this month, the firm's portfolio company Perforce Software Inc. acquired Perfecto Mobile Ltd., a provider of cloud-based mobile and web application testing software.

The funds published on these pages are grouped together by fund management company.

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in theUK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors .

Single price: Based onamid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available .

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge fromfund managers/operators.

Features
Government puts effort into quest to make 20 unicorns by 2023

Leo Lewis 
896 words
22 October 2018
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Asia
2
English
Copyright 2018 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Start-ups Shinzo Abe has pledged to help double venture capital investment, says Leo Lewis

On October 15, four months after making history by raising $1.2bn in its initial public offering, Mercari was doing exactly what investors hoped it would. The company   which had already gained recognition for becoming Japan's first unicorn, defined as a start-up valued at over $1bn   was busily selling stuff on its app.

Items for sale on Mercari's e-commerce portal that day included a pink and blue pair of ecigarette starter kits, a pair of scuffed children's rain boots and an unopened tube of Yves Saint Laurent all-in-one foundation. Mundane, perhaps, but the site has created a trading forum that did not exist before and Japan has learnt, through Mercari, that the country is capable of producing a tech unicorn.

The question now, for investors, government and Japan's would-be unicorn breeders of the future, is whether it can produce more. For the government of prime minister Shinzo Abe, it is an especially potent issue. His administration wants to spur venture capital investment relative to gross domestic product to twice its current levels by 2022 and help create 20 unicorn start-ups by 2023. Achieving that goal will be a big challenge, say analysts at Mizuho Securities, who add that the pipeline of potential new unicorns is almost empty.

The venture capital market in Japan has historically been tiny and remains small despite recent increases. At the same time, the country cannot rely on existing corporates to re-allocate capital internally to create the next unicorn and spin it out.

Mercari's experience is not one that can be used as a template for other wannabe tech start-ups. The company is exceptional for a number of reasons, not least of which is that it already had a fully functioning business model before it began trading on Japan's Mothers market for start-ups. It had been able to develop before it came to market because its business required very little capital. Other companies are likely to need much more VC nurturing before they come to market, experts say. Many of the companies that list on Mothers, whose "wild west" reputation is beloved by retail investors, are so much less prepared that they have helped endow the market with its notorious volatility. Breakthroughs in biotech, AI or robotics tend to flash into the spotlight and then fail to materialise. Kabutan, a website for retail investors, tracks the investment themes and their daily cycle in and out of the market's attention. At the time of writing, the top slots were held by automated shop checkout technology, 5G and QR codes.

There are positive signs, however: "Compared to five years ago," says Kabutan analyst Hideyuki Okari, "investor interest in start-ups [is] very high."

But with the buzz of its unicorn trailblazing now worn off, Mercari's shares have followed a familiar pattern to other Mothers names: the shares surged 77 per cent on their opening day, but fell sharply immediately afterwards and have never recovered those highs. That decline comes against a background where Mothers itself is suffering one of its periodical crises of confidence. The index that tracks a basket of its representative stocks has fallen more than 40 per cent since June 2017.

The problem is that, while Japan's still fledgling VC market attempts to grow, the Mothers market is being forced to perform the function that venture capital would be doing elsewhere, but with much less patience. It may be a while before the gap is filled. According to Japan's Venture Enterprise Center, Japanese venture investment in fiscal 2017 amounted to 135.4bn ($1.2bn)   a significant 24 per cent increase from the previous year but still minuscule compared with the US or China.

As analysts at Morgan Stanley highlighted in a recent report. Japan's spending on research and development and venture capital may be increasing, but investment amounts are still small compared to international counterparts such as the US and China. They also say Japanese funding tends to be geared to final product manufacturing processes and components, an area where it is much harder to create unicorns. Nevertheless, the same analysts presented a research paper in September in which they named 100 Japanese companies, listed and unlisted, that are turning disruption and innovation into opportunities. "Life sciences and healthcare are the largest categories for [Japanese start-up] selections," said the report: "companies involved in AI, IoT, robotics and big data account for about a third."

Having set concrete targets, the government is now trying to speed things up. The Ministry of Economy, Trade and Industry established the J-Start-up programme in June this year to help support promising young companies with its vision of creating 20 unicorns.

The top-down approach has not impressed some observers, who think the government may not be the best judge of where the next unicorns will come from. For example, a public-private sector forum is planned for launch later this year with the aim of making Japan the first country in the world to create flying cars. "This is probably not very realistic," Mizuho Securities chief strategist Masatoshi Kikuchi said in a research note.
